Experian up 3% despite FX moves erasing growth

Joel Lewin 
312 words
15 January 2016
09:15
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Shares in Experian climbed as much as 3 per cent this morning as the credit checker racked up some decent growth in North America in the third quarter, although currency moves wiped out the group s overall sales growth, highlighting the heavy impact the greenback s rally is having on dollar earnings.

Total revenue fell 3 per cent in the three months to December, but stripping out currency moves it rose 6 per cent. Including the impact of currency moves, sales dropped in every region except North America, but without currency moves, sales rose in every region.

In Latin America, sales rose 6 per cent ignoring currency moves, whereas reported sales fell 29 per cent, underscoring the drag exerted by the decline in emerging market currencies.

The group rather darkly explained its success in Brazil:

In Brazil, we benefited from growth across both consumer and business information, including an increasing contribution from delinquency notifications linked to higher volumes of non-performing loans.

In October the company "http://on.ft.com/1NWO79J" at one of its US servers, affecting T-Mobile.

Brian Cassin, chief executive, said:

Growth was balanced, with all four regions contributing within our targeted mid-single digit range. Strategically, we divested a number of smaller assets to further focus the portfolio and we are expanding rapidly in the key areas of Credit Services and Decision Analytics. In North America Consumer Services, we continue to see underlying improvement in trends and benefited in the quarter from one-off revenue for on-boarding of a new affinity client.

Looking ahead, our guidance for the full year is unchanged. While foreign exchange will be a headwind, at constant currency we expect organic revenue growth in the mid-single digit range, to deliver stable margins and to see further progress in Benchmark earnings per share.

Property Report: Builders Bet on Booming Rents

By Laura Kusisto 
1,034 words
13 January 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright  2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

After six years of rising apartment rents in U.S. cities, investors from all corners of the real-estate industry are piling into new projects in a bet that the boom still has a long way to run.

Over the next three years, developers are expected to build almost one million apartments in the U.S., more than the nearly 900,000 constructed over the previous three, according to researcher Axiometrics Inc.

In 2014, multifamily rental construction reached 328,000 units, its highest in nearly 30 years, according to an analysis of U.S. Census data by Jed Kolko, a senior fellow at the Terner Center for Housing Innovation at the University of California, Berkeley.

The main lure for investors: rising rents. Average rents nationwide rose 4.6% in 2015, the biggest gain since before the recession, according to real-estate researcher Reis Inc. Rents have increased by more than 20% since the beginning of 2010. Most economists expect 2016 to be another strong year. The average monthly U.S. apartment rent now stands at nearly $1,180, up from about $1,125 a year ago, according to Reis.

"I sound to myself like a broken record because I've been saying the same thing for quite a few years now," said Mark Obrinsky, chief economist at the National Multifamily Housing Council. "Demand remains high for apartments."

Sales of U.S. multifamily apartment properties clocked in at a record $138.7 billion in 2015, up just over 30% from a year earlier, according to JLL, a commercial-real-estate-services firm. Last year, 4,915 multifamily projects were sold, up from 4,570 in 2014.

But some real-estate analysts worry that most of the new construction has been aimed at the top 20% of the market. In all, 82% of the units built from 2012 to 2014 in 54 major U.S. metropolitan areas are classified as luxury developments, according to CoStar Group Inc.

"What concerns me is not so much the volume of construction but that there's an intense focus on building the same thing: luxury properties in urban locations," said Jay Parsons, director of analytics for MPF Research, a division of RealPage Inc., who has been advising clients to look more at vibrant suburban markets.

The shift toward luxury is putting renters near the limit of what they can afford.

In downtown Los Angeles, new one-bedroom properties are commanding rents in the range of $3,000 a month. According to CoStar, a tenant would need a gross income of $128,000 to afford one of those units, four times the median household income in the area of just over $29,000 a year.

In Boston's trendy Leather District, renters would need to make nearly $142,000 to afford average rents for newly built one-bedroom apartments of more than $3,500 a month, according to CoStar. The median income in the area is closer to $60,000.

Despite the affordability issues, apartments are drawing interest from a broad swath of real-estate companies, including home builders that haven't traditionally built multifamily housing. Toll Brothers Inc., primarily a single-family home builder, has 7,400 apartment units planned or under development, and company executives say that could double over the next couple of years.

"All these trends supporting apartment demand are going to be with us for the foreseeable future," said Charles Elliott, managing director of Toll Brothers Apartment Living.

Major office landlords, such as Mack-Cali Realty Corp. also are focusing more on apartments. The company, which was solely an office landlord before 2010, through acquisition and development has amassed 7,000 units and plans by 2019 to more than double that to 15,000 units.

"Most people forming households today are not buying," said Michael DeMarco, president of Mack-Cali.

Urban markets are especially hot. The number of apartments completed in 30 major downtown city centers hit more than 54,000 for 2015, according to an estimate by Axiometrics, a 20-year high.

Toby Bozzuto, president and chief executive of the Bozzuto Group, a Washington-area builder, said he has seen an influx in the past few years of newcomers that once specialized only in office or retail but now are dabbling in apartments. He remains bullish on the apartment market, but has concerns that investors are piling on and driving up land prices beyond what rents can support.

Los Angeles-based Jamison, one of the city's largest office landlords, is taking its first plunge into residential property. For two decades the company specialized in owning no-frills office buildings in the Koreatown neighborhood. Now its Jamison Properties LP unit is diving headlong into apartments, with 27 projects under development or in the planning stages.

About a third of the new units are conversions of the company's existing buildings, while the rest are new construction, including Jamison's most ambitious project, two 35-story towers in downtown Los Angeles with 648 luxury units.

Jaime Lee, CEO of Jamison Realty Inc., expects the market will start to slow in the next few years, so Jamison is breaking ground on some projects as soon as possible.

"People are working against the clock right now," she said. "We're coming to market as quickly as we can."

Some signs of strain are beginning to emerge. The vacancy rate for downtown apartment buildings in U.S. cities jumped to 6.1% in the third quarter of 2015 from 4.4% in the first quarter of 2013, according to Reis. During the same period, the suburban vacancy rate fell to 4.1% from 4.4% -- suggesting some renters are leaving high-price inner-city areas and moving farther out. New projects in the third quarter were 49% unleased upon completion, according to CoStar Group Inc., up from 29% in the third quarter of 2014.

"With all the new product being created skewed to luxury . . . we're building lots of rental product but it doesn't necessarily match up with what the demand really is," said New York-based appraiser Jonathan Miller.

U.S. News: Financial Crisis Still Splits Parties

By Nick Timiraos 
711 words
12 January 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
Copyright  2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The 2016 campaign is offering ample evidence for why Washington, eight years after the financial crisis, remains so divided on how to regulate Wall Street: Republicans and Democrats still have wildly different interpretations about what caused the bust.

GOP candidates fault government policies and low interest rates for fueling the housing bubble that preceded the crisis. They strongly support easing financial regulations passed in the aftermath, singling out the 2010 Dodd-Frank rules signed into law by President Barack Obama.

Democrats say the crisis was fueled by private-sector and regulatory failures, not government policy. But they are divided over the need to break up big banks and the role of a 1999 law signed by President Bill Clinton that repealed parts of the Glass-Steagall Act, a New Deal-era statute that separated commercial and investment banking. The fight has put front-runner Hillary Clinton on the defensive by highlighting broader concerns over her willingness to stand up to the financial lobby.

Economists say many of the proposals floated by candidates fall short, to the degree that they turn on oversimplifications of what drove the bust. "In this kind of environment, you want a short, punchy message to be effective," said Douglas Holtz-Eakin, who is president of a conservative think tank and advised Sen. John McCain during his GOP presidential run in 2008. Such catchphrases, he said, don't always translate into good policy.

In debates, GOP candidates have blamed the crisis partly on the Federal Reserve , which they say maintained low rates for too long, and on Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac , the government-backed mortgage-finance firms that effectively set lending standards for much of the housing market.

They also say the crisis response did too little to address large banks and saddled small banks that didn't play major roles in the crisis with a punitive compliance burden.

In a November debate, businesswoman Carly Fiorina said the law provided a "great example of how socialism starts," because the government, having created a problem by overpromoting homeownership, then stepped in to solve it.

Critics of Dodd-Frank say a bigger problem is that the law doesn't do enough to solve the problem of firms being too complex. They are also wary because the law assumes a lack of regulation -- rather than the unwillingness to use regulatory powers that existed -- is what drove the crisis.

"Republicans have legitimate criticisms, but you have to propose some sensible alternatives" to Dodd-Frank, such as improving the mechanism for how failing banks are liquidated, said Mr. Holtz-Eakin, whom GOP lawmakers appointed to a congressional panel investigating the crisis. "I don't see how you can say, 'We'll just get rid of Dodd-Frank.' " An additional hurdle, he said, is that Democrats have reflexively resisted even technical changes to the bill "regardless of the merit."

Fannie and Freddie used their lobbying clout to resist tougher capital standards before the boom, but the loans they backed still performed better than mortgages packaged into securities by Wall Street firms.

From 2003 to 2006, the period in which the housing bubble inflated most dramatically, the share of loans backed by Fannie and Freddie fell from more than half to around a third, according to Inside Mortgage Finance, a newsletter. Crisis-era loans packaged into securities by Fannie and Freddie have experienced realized loss rates of around 3%, compared with 23% for those packaged into securities by Wall Street, according to Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics.

For Mrs. Clinton, restoring Glass-Steagall wouldn't do enough to oversee large insurers and hedge funds. "I just don't think it would get the job done," she said at a Democratic debate in November.

Critics of Wall Street deregulation say her answer undersold the role global banks had in extending credit to lightly regulated nonbank firms that fueled the housing bubble. "I knew when you merge large insurance companies and investment banks and commercial banks, it was not going to be good," said White House hopeful and Vermont Sen. Bernie Sanders, who as a House member voted against the 1999 Glass-Steagall repeal.

COMPANIES
Small energy groups covered in red ink; Oil & gas

By Kiran Stacey, Energy Correspondent 
471 words
11 January 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
21
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Survey of Aim-listed oil companies highlights impact of oil price plunge

Nine out of 10 small London-listed oil and gas companies are making losses, according to new data highlighting the full impact of the plunging crude price on junior energy groups.

A survey of 104 exploration and production companies listed on the London Stock Exchange's Aim market by Company Watch, a research firm, found that 42 per cent were in its "warning area" at the end of last year.

This means that the groups are 50 times more likely to suffer financial distress than a typical company, according to Company Watch. It highlights how energy groups' efforts to cut costs to cope with the oil price rout have not been universally successful.

Company Watch found the combined market value of the 104 companies had dropped by 40 per cent in the past year. Ninety per cent were making pre-tax losses in their most recent accounts, but the overall performance was better than in 2014 partly because costs have been cut significantly.

Ewan Mitchell, head of analytics at Company Watch, said: "Smaller oil and gas companies on Aim will remain under severe pressure because fundraising in this environment will be testing, and many will have already cut costs to the bone last year with little room for more this year. "

The energy industry has been hit hard over the past 18 months, as the price of Brent crude has slumped from $115 a barrel to below $40 - the lowest level in more than a decade.

Smaller oil and gas companies have fared particularly badly, with many having taken on large amounts of debt as they aggressively pushed for new finds while the oil price was still high.

The Company Watch data show that the 104 companies' combined gross debt has remained stubbornly high, at 2bn at the time of these groups' last accounts - a similar level to the same time in 2014.

The data also highlight the broader stresses being placed on North Sea operators listed outside of the UK. Iona Energy, the UK subsidiary of a Canadian listed company, last week announced it had called in administrators to help dismantle its operations.

Iona had hoped to sell an interest in its Orlando field, to the east of Shetland, but a prospective buyer pulled out, leaving the company with little option but to begin insolvency procedures.

Atlantic Petroleum, a Faroese company listed in Denmark and Iceland, which owns 19 North Sea production licences, is also struggling, having missed a debt repayment in December.

PA Resources, an explorer listed in Sweden that focuses on the North Sea and Africa, announced last year that it would liquidate its assets after failing to secure funding.

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - LIFE & ARTS
FT Diary

By Louise Chunn 
949 words
9 January 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
02
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

I have recently returned from Blackbox, a tech accelerator course in Palo Alto, California, where start-ups can learn from venture capitalists, established founders and others about the way Silicon Valley operates. There isn't a better place to take a magical mystery tour of the future of digital. This small city of 65,000 people is the heart of the Valley, where Facebook, Google and Apple all started out.

The two-week residential programme, sponsored by Google for Entrepreneurs, is a hot ticket. I was the only British founder among a group of 19 gathered from all over the world. Depending on our mood, the atmosphere either resembled a Coke advert or a UN conclave from Uganda and Uruguay, Bangalore and Berlin. My first conversation was with a founder from the West Bank who had teamed up with a younger man from Tel Aviv. They almost always sat together chatting, one seeking the perfect line to explain his complex platform, the other receiving help with his business plan. The Palestinian quietly admitted that such closeness would be harder back on home territory.

Just four days into our stay, a young radicalised Muslim couple killed 14 people in another part of California; a few days later, Republican presidential hopeful Donald Trump was calling for the US to ban all Muslims from coming into the country. By my calculations, such a law would have excluded just under a third of our group, including two charming young Pakistanis who, still in their early twenties, had come up with a watch to help people afflicted by tremors to analyse their condition - Trequant is a potentially life-changing device that could help up to 6m sufferers in the US alone. Among our group, neither the attack nor Trump ever came up in conversation.

Just over two years ago, Aileen Lee, a US investor, coined the term "unicorn" to describe tech companies valued at more than $1bn before their initial public offering. At last count there were 144 of these, topped by Uber at $51bn.

This is how everyone wants to make it big now. The people at Google might talk about getting your product right, building it slowly, but there's more than a whiff of the gold rush about Silicon Valley these days. People speak of a bubble that's about to burst; others simply point out that most of the big, problem-solving ideas have been done.

Keith Teare, co-founder of the website TechCrunch and now heading an investment and consultancy business, told us that unicorn-hunting is a reality, albeit a rather unpalatable one. The chances of such huge success are pitifully low, so investment is increasingly hard to come by. "You're trying to move investors from fear to greed. If you can't ignite their greed then you won't get a cheque. Risk only affects the price they'll pay."

Mike Maples, a venture capitalist who told us he made 500 times his seed investment in Twitter, laid out some harsh statistics for our group: "Ninety-seven per cent of the wealth created in Silicon Valley was made by 10 companies. Do you really want to sacrifice five years of your life for the scraps of three per cent? I love founders and want them to do well, but it's a very tough job."

Maples was charismatic, with a low boredom threshold. I told him he reminded me of an old-style record company A&R man, only searching for talent in the digital new world. It was an assessment he seemed to agree with: "I always said I wanted to be to tech like Berry Gordy was to Motown."

My start-up, Welldoing.org, helps people find the most suitable therapist for their needs. There are more than 1,000 different modes of therapy; people can be overwhelmed by the choice and simply give up. I'm aware that this subject is not at the forefront for the A+ programmers of Silicon Valley - currently preoccupied by AI (artificial intelligence), robotics, the Internet of Things and virtual reality - but mental health is a big issue here.

Founder depression is an acknowledged syndrome and there have been a number of well-publicised suicides. Experts blame the toxic mix of long hours (many talk of 80-hour weeks) and little sleep but surely the clincher is the huge competition and pressure to be first to market and then to make it large as quickly as you possibly can. Anything less is failure.

With tech, super-quick growth is possible - one of our talks at Blackbox was on the subject of exponentiality from Pascal Finette at Silicon Valley's Singularity University, a think-tank set up in 2008 to solve the world's most critical problems through technology. But, at the same time, start-ups are burning through money, so investors get antsy, or someone else comes along with the same idea and a shorter timescale.

One of the many founders we heard from was an eminently sane chap from Poland, Marcin Treder of the design application UXPin. "When we were raising seed investment there was nothing else in my life. No matter what your friends do, you're not going to Yosemite." But once he got his money, his venture capitalist introduced him to a monthly entrepreneurs' meeting. "We get together and we talk about our problems. It's like group therapy."

In the valley of the unicorns, sharing your troubles may not make you rich. But it's probably the best way to stay sane.

Louise Chunn is the founder of welldoing.org

WSJ.D Technology: Apple Purchases Startup Emotient

By Rolfe Winkler, Daisuke Wakabayashi and Elizabeth Dwoskin 
402 words
8 January 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B3
English
(Copyright (c) 2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Apple Inc. has purchased Emotient Inc., a startup that uses artificial-intelligence technology to read people's emotions by analyzing facial expressions.

It isn't clear what Apple plans to do with Emotient's technology, which primarily was sold to advertisers to help assess viewer reactions to their ads. Doctors also have tested it to interpret signs of pain among patients unable to express themselves, and a retailer used it to monitor shoppers' facial expressions, the company had said.

Improving image recognition is a hot topic in Silicon Valley, where Apple rivals Facebook Inc., Alphabet Inc.'s Google and others are investing heavily in artificial-intelligence techniques.

An Apple spokeswoman confirmed the purchase, but declined to elaborate.

Emotient, based in San Diego, previously had raised $8 million from investors, including Intel Capital. The company had been seeking a new round of venture-capital financing, but wasn't able to secure it on favorable terms according to a person familiar with the matter.

Emotient Chief Executive Ken Denman declined to comment. The company this week revised its website and removed details about the services it had been selling.

Apple has expressed interest in the field. In a 2014 patent application, it described a software system that would analyze and identify people's moods based on a variety of clues, including facial expression.

In October, Apple confirmed that it had acquired another artificial-intelligence startup, VocalIQ Ltd., which aims to improve a computer's ability to understand natural speech.

In May, Emotient announced that it had been granted a patent for a method of collecting and labeling as many as 100,000 facial images a day so computers can better recognize different expressions.

Its technology leaves some skittish, including Paul Ekman, a psychologist who pioneered the study of reading faces to determine emotions and is an adviser to Emotient.

In an interview with The Wall Street Journal last January, Dr. Ekman said he was torn between the potential power of software that can read emotions and the need to ensure that it doesn't infringe on personal privacy. He said the technology could reveal people's emotions without their consent, and their feelings could be misinterpreted.

An Emotient spokeswoman says the company doesn't reveal information about individuals, only aggregate data.

Job Moves: who is hiring and where

By FT reporters 
3,317 words
6 January 2016
12:01
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The latest job moves in the trading industry and coverage of people making the news in the world s financial capitals

Wall retires from chief executive role at Marex Spectron

John Wall is to retire as chief executive of Marex Spectron after more than three years at the helm amid a wide-ranging board reshuffle at the UK commodities broker.

As part of the changes Jeremy Isaacs, the group s non-executive chairman and a key figure in the creation of the Marex business, will also step back from his role.

The duo have played a key role in short life of the company, whose modern incarnation was created when Marex bought by JRJ Group, a private equity firm founded by Mr Isaacs, former head of Lehman s European operations. In the intervening six years it has grown rapidly by hiring staff and purchasing smaller brokers, such as the 94.5m deal for Spectron, in 2011. Mr Wall, who had been at UBS for more than 25 years until his appointment at Marex, was appointed in part to integrate the Marex and Spectron companies.

Mr Wall will be replaced by Ian Lowitt, the group s chief financial officer, who will also move from New York to London to take over. Rob Watts, the group s financial controller, will become Marex s new CFO.

Both appointments take effect from Friday 8th January and remain subject to regulatory approval.

 With the firm in excellent shape, I feel that now is the right time for me to step back. It has been a privilege to lead this great company and I have no doubt that Ian is the right person to take charge of the next stage of its development,  said Mr Wall.

Mr Isaacs will be succeeded by Simon Heale, a non-executive director and former chief executive of the London Metal Exchange. Mr Isaacs will remain on the board. (Philip Stafford, January 6)

Euronext appoints former Tullet executive to lead FICC push

Euronext has appointed Paul Humphrey, a former executive at interdealer broker Tullett Prebon, to help the European exchanges operator develop a strategy to exploit rapidly-changing fixed income, rates and foreign exchange markets.

Mr Humphrey, who had been working at Thomson Reuters as a consultant, will join the group on January 4 with the title of head of fixed income, rates and FX (FICC) strategy. He was previously chief executive of Tullett s electronic broking and information business until the summer of 2014. He has also worked at ABN Amro, Van der Moolen, SLK/Goldman Sachs and ICAP.

His appointment comes as exchanges are increasingly eyeing markets that have long been dominated by banks, which continue to make billions of dollars in profits from it each year. Exchanges have generally been unable to establish a presence in fixed income and forex markets. However a combination of unlawful activity by banks, deep structural change and the emergence of cheap and reliable technology that has allowed alternative ways of trading to emerge. Euronext operates stock, commodity and derivatives markets around the region but has little OTC business.

Mr Humphrey will report to Lee Hodgkinson, head of markets and global sales, who said:  His appointment further strengthens our senior leadership team and his vast experience and strong vision will help us fulfil our growth ambitions in an important sector of the business.  (Philip Stafford, December 2)

Preuss to step away from Eurex role in Deutsche Brse reorganisation

Deutsche Brse, Europe s largest exchanges operator by market capitalisation, has unveiled a wide-ranging internal reorganisation that will see Andreas Preuss step away from his role as head of the Eurex derivatives exchange.

The internal reshaping comes five months after Carsten Kengeter, a former UBS banker, became chief executive with a plan to  put client focus at the heart of its structure.  In a statement on Wednesday, Deutsche Brse said it would create a new  Clients, Products and Core Markets  unit that will encompass its derivatives trading, clearing and settlement business. It will be headed by Jeffrey Tessler, chief executive of its Clearstream settlement business.

Mr Preuss, deputy chief executive and for many years Deutsche Brse s most senior representative at industry events, will become chief executive of a new division, called  IT & Operations, Data & New Asset Classes . It will combine Deutsche Brse  market data unit, the European Energy Exchange and 360T, the foreign exchange trading network the company bought for  ¬725m over the summer.

The group s cash equities business, which includes the well-known Frankfurt Stock Exchange Xetra, will be separated from the Eurex derivatives business and given its own divisional status  given its high political as well as macroeconomic importance , the company said. The enlarged division, named  Cash Market, Pre-IPO & Growth Financing , will include certificates and warrant businesses and the creation of an  innovative pre-IPO market . It will be led by Hauke Stars, who was previously responsible for Deutsche Brse s market data division and the renewal of its IT infrastructure.

Mr Kengeter will be directly responsible for group strategy, mergers and acquisitions as well as government relations. (Philip Stafford, November 12)

London Stock Exchange Group appoints MSCI s Corrado as COO

London Stock Exchange Group has appointed Chris Corrado from MSCI as both group chief operating officer and chief information officer to replace the departing Antoine Shagoury.

The appointment is effective immediately. Mr Corrado, who has also held a series of senior technology officer roles at banks like Morgan Stanley, Deutsche Bank, Merrill Lynch and UBS as well as companies outside financial services, including eBay and AT&T Wireless. He began his career at IBM.

Mr Shagoury is returning to his family in the US.  I d like to extend my warmest sincere thanks to Antoine for his leadership and outstanding contribution to the Group over the past five years. Antoine has identified a strong successor and developed a deep bench of talent to carry on his legacy,  said Xavier Rolet, LSE group chief executive.  I m confident the Group will benefit from Chris  deep experience as we continue to diversify our business and grow our global footprint. (Philip Stafford, November 12)

London Stock Exchange Group hires former CME lobbyist Jachym

Jonathan Jachym, the former CME Group senior lobbyist in Washington, has re-emerged in a similar role for the London Stock Exchange Group as the US becomes an increasing focus of business for the UK bourse.

Mr Jachym quit the CME at the start of September after five years in the role to  pursue another opportunity , as he described in a departing email. He was previously executive director, government relations at CME in Washington and held a similar position in Europe for three years.

Mr Jachym will become head of North America regulatory strategy and government relations for the LSE, according to two people familiar with the move.

His appointment comes as North American market assumes a greater role in the overall LSE business. Its $2.7bn purchase of Frank Russell, the index compiler, has meant that around 30 per cent of turnover comes from the US. To underscore that focus, this year the LSE has also licensed a series of Russell and FTSE indices to CME Group, the world s largest futures exchange, to develop equity index futures and options. It agreed a similar deal with another Chicago exchange, CBOE, in February. Mary Schapiro, who was chair of the Securities Exchange Commission between 2009 to 2012, also joined the LSE s board on July 1.

Before that, Mr Jachym was legal and regulatory counsel at the Center for Capital Markets Competitiveness, a US consumer trade group. (Philip Stafford, October 9)

London Stock Exchange Group names new head of exchange

The London Stock Exchange Group, which runs exchanges, indices and trading platforms, has promoted Nikhil Rathi to be the chief executive of its stock exchange business, the London Stock Exchange.

He will replace Alexander Justham, known through the industry as  JJ , who informed the group of his decision to leave earlier this year after three years in the role, the bourse said in a statement.

Mr Justham previously had responsibility for regulatory strategy, government and public affairs, and UK regulation, and formerly worked at the UK financial regulator, the-then Financial Services Authority.

Xavier Rolet, group chief executive of the LSE, said:

 I would like to warmly extend my personal thanks to JJ for his great contribution and wish him all the best for the future. 

Mr Rathi joined the LSE from the UK Treasury last year to be its chief of staff and director of international development. His roles at the finance department included director of the financial services group representing the UK government s interests in the EU and internationally.

 During the past year, Nikhil has played a key role driving the Group s strategy in key markets, such as China and India as well as supporting key integration projects across the group,  Mr Rolet added. (Philip Stafford, September 29)

Deutsche Brse appoints Gotsman as derivatives adviser

Ilan Gotsman, one of Europe s leading futures trading entrepreneurs, has joined Deutsche Brse as an adviser to develop its Eurex derivatives business.

The 51-year old Israeli was appointed with immediate effect, the German exchange told employees in a memo released on Tuesday.

His role will be to advise Eurex on ways to increase both volume and open interest in contracts, product development, pricing structures and improve its competitive position. He will also advise on identifying other growth opportunities, the company said.

He will report to Carsten Kengeter, the group s new chief executive, and Andreas Preuss, deputy chief executive and head of Eurex.

Mr Gotsman has maintained a low public profile but is well-known within the futures industry. In the early 1990s he helped to set up GH Financials, a London-based clearing broker.

He has also founded Butterfly Investments, which uses proprietary mathematical models to identify mispricing in the exchange-trade interest rate derivative. He also founded Matrix Capital Markets Ltd, a private trading derivative company in 2003. (Philip Stafford, September 8)

Markit names Mutnikas as co-head of Solutions business

Markit, the UK data provider, has appointed a former Bank of England risk adviser to help develop its fast-growing analytics business.

The London-based group named Yaacov Mutnikas as managing director and co-head of its Solutions business, it said in a statement. He reports to Lance Uggla, chief executive, and also joins Markit's executive team. He began last Thursday.

"I want analytics to be growing force in Markit in the future," said Mr Uggla. "When you think about the use of technology, the quantitative use of risk, in margin for collateral and the pieces of the post 2008 world, there is a lot of analysis required. It needs an analytic mind but also someone comfortable with technology and software. Yaacov has the toolbox to make a big difference."

Turnover at the unit rose 17 per cent to $82.3m in the three months to June 30, driven by new business wins and acquisitions. Excluding deals, Markit s Solutions business has recorded organic growth of 13 per cent in the first six months of the year.

Mr Mutnikas has worked as a senior adviser to the Bank of England on systemic risk. He has also worked at Bridgewater Associates, a hedge fund, and risk management software business Algorithmics, where he was chief technology officer. It was sold to IBM for $387m in 2011.

He is also a keen rower and was part of the team that holds the record for the fastest 8-man crossing of the Atlantic. (Philip Stafford, September 7)

DTCC names Scharf as chief security officer

DTCC, the US post-trade services provider, has appointed former Bloomberg and Experian executive Stephen Scharf to the newly-created position of chief security officer.

The move will allow Mark Clancy, also DTCC chief information security officer, to become the full-time head of a venture to combat threats from computer hacking in financial markets.

Mr Scharf was previously global chief information security officer at Experian, the credit data scoring company.

The group said Mr Clancy will be able to assume his full-time position as chief executive of Soltra, the project between the DTCC and the financial services industry to encourage banks and other critical infrastructure companies to improve the sharing of information on cyber attacks.

 Risk management remains a top priority across the industry, and we look forward to Stephen s contributions as we continue to protect DTCC and the global markets,  said Andrew Gray, managing director, group chief risk officer at DTCC. (Philip Stafford, July 21)

WFE names FCA s Hill to key policy role

The World Federation of Exchanges, a trade association for the world s bourses, has hired Gavin Hill from the UK s main markets watchdog as its new head of regulatory affairs.

Mr Hill spent 14 years at the Financial Conduct Authority and its predecessors, and has overseen equities, derivatives and commodities exchanges since 2006 as a manager in its Markets and Supervision Divisions. He was also responsible for the FCA s commodity markets policy and served on committees for the Euronext College of Regulators.

The 50-year old organisation has moved to London and reshuffled its staff to bolster its role as an active lobbyist, compared to a traditional focus on market data and industry research.

 The addition of Gavin will significantly strengthen the WFE team and reinforce its regulatory capacity and institutional knowledge as well as its City of London stakeholder network at a key time for the organisation,  said Nandini Sukumar, the new chief executive of the WFE.

Mr Hill starts at the WFE on September 21. (Philip Stafford, July 7)

LCH.Clearnet names Vinci as head of US repo unit

LCH.Clearnet, the world s largest over-the-counter swaps clearer, has appointed John Vinci from BNY Mellon to spearhead a push into the US fixed income clearing business.

Mr Vinci, who will become Head of US RepoClear, will be responsible for developing the clearing house s business in a critical part of daily market operations.

Repo markets grease the wheels of the financial system by allowing bonds to be exchanged for cash on the proviso that they will be repurchased at a later date. At present LCH largely clears cash bond and repo trades across 13 European markets. Monthly volumes average around  ¬13tn.

 This new appointment will support the business  growth ambitions in North America, working with our clients and members to deliver greater efficiencies in the US fixed income markets,  said David Weisbrod, chief executive of LCH s US business.

Mr Vinci will start on July 13 and report to Mr Weisbrod and Bruce Kellaway, global head of fixed income at LCH. He has also worked at JPMorgan Chase and Bankers Trust.(Philip Stafford, July 7)

LSE appoints former SEC chair Schapiro to its board

Mary Schapiro, one of the US s most experienced markets regulators, will join the board of the London Stock Exchange Group as a non-executive director.

Her appointment reflects the LSE s focus on US stock and derivatives markets for growth. The group is hoping to capitalise on two trends   greater use of passive investments like exchange traded funds and a regulatory mandate to risk manage more over-the-counter swaps through clearing houses.

Ms Schapiro, who was chair of the Securities Exchange Commission between 2009 to 2012, will join on July 1. She has also been chair of the Commodity Futures Trading Commission, the US derivatives regulator, and the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority (Finra).

 Mary s extensive knowledge of the US financial and regulatory landscape, her broad global markets experience, and her understanding of complex financial infrastructure will be of great value to the group as we continue to grow our global footprint,  said Chris Gibson-Smith, LSE chairman.

Last year the LSE bought index compiler Frank Russell Company from insurer Northwestern Mutual   its largest deal to date   to expand into indices and exchange traded funds. More than $5tn in assets are benchmarked to Russell indices, which include the well-known Russell 2000 for listed small-cap US companies.

The LSE also created a new role, head of its US business, to be held by David Weisbrod. Mr Weisbrod will be responsible for implementing the LSE s plans for the region and also co-ordinate its relationship with its main regulators and other public stakeholders.

He will continue in his role as chief executive of the US business of LCH.Clearnet, the world s largest swaps clearing house. (Philip Stafford, June 11)

NYSE appoints Cunningham as new COO

The New York Stock Exchange has promoted Stacey Cunningham, once a floor trader at the Big Board, to be its new chief operating officer.

In her new role, Ms Cunningham will oversee the rollout of a new trading technology platform, called Pillar, and the introduction of NYSE's planned midday auction, which is intended to boost liquidity in thinly-traded securities.

Ms Cunningham, previously president of the exchange's governance division, will report to Tom Farley, NYSE Group president. She has also worked as head of sales and relationship management for NYSE. After leaving trading a decade ago, she also worked at Nasdaq.

 Stacey will contribute to our work on enhancing and simplifying our equities platforms, advocating for listed companies and our trading customers in market structure discussions, and innovating to deliver more value to the global capital markets,  said Mr Farley. (Philip Stafford, June 3)

Hasenpusch set to take over at CME Clearing Europe

CME Group, the world s largest futures operator, is set for a change of head at its European clearing business.

Lee Betsil, the current head of CME Clearing Europe, is expected to return to the US over the summer and the Chicago-based group plans to replace him with Tina Hasenpusch, the unit s current chief operating officer, according to two people familiar with the situation. Ms Hasnpusch s appointment is dependent on approval by UK regulators, the people said.

CME began clearing off-exchange interest rate swaps to challenge the market incumbent LCH.Clearnet for a share of the $500tn business two years ago, and has expanded to clear commodity, power and energy futures traded on its London-based exchange.

Its clearing house is also testing changes to the way it calculates the margin required to back interest rate swaps and will provide estimates for the impact on open positions to its clearing members later this year. (Philip Stafford, May 21)

Virtu appoints former ICAP Nixon to board

Virtu Financial, the US high-speed electronic market maker, has appointed former ICAP executive John Nixon to its board of directors, effective immediately.

Mr Nixon, who was most recently head of ICAP s US business, left the UK interdealer broker in March after a 16 year career in which he held a range of senior positions.

He had overseen ICAP s fixed income electronic broking business in the US, which covered some of the group s most important revenue-generating desks. His period in charge included the creation of ICAP s electronic over-the-counter interest rate derivatives platform, called iSwap, as well as its new swap execution facility, the new electronic marketplaces mandated by US regulators as they seek to strengthen global OTC markets.

Virtu lifted the lid on some modern high-speed trading practices when it floated on Nasdaq last month, with an equity market capitalisation of just under 3bn.

 I have known John during his successful career at ICAP and always relied on his integrity and judgment. We look forward to benefiting from his insights on the board as well as his deep and long term relationships,  said Vinnie Viola, Virtu s executive chairman, in a statement. (Philip Stafford, May 5)

Business
Family Leave Gaining Momentum in the Workplace; Big changes at blue-chip companies last year changed life for a lot of new parents

By Rachel Emma Silverman 
717 words
6 January 2016
00:41
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Big changes at blue-chip companies last year changed life for a lot of new parents, but there is still a long way to go.

Amazon.com Inc., Microsoft Corp., Accenture, Netflix Inc., Blackstone Group LP, Nestl SA and others have expanded paid leave for new parents and added benefits such as shipping breast milk and paying for family nannies to travel with employees on business trips.

At Netflix, employees can take up to a year of paid leave to care for a child, while Amazon also increased time off for fathers and adoptive parents, in addition to birth mothers.

Benefits experts say that trend will continue in 2016, as companies court millennial employees who are beginning to start families and seeking roles that allow greater flexibility.

Experts also expect to see more workers thinking beyond leave, figuring out ways employees can balance the demands of work and family after they return to the job. Accenture allows new parents to cut back on business travel during that first year, and private-equity firm KKR & Co. and investment bank Credit Suisse Group AG now pay for infants and child care providers to travel with new parents who have to be on the road.

The big question in 2016 will be whether new parents will take those extended leaves and what happens when they return to the office.

 It's one thing to offer a benefit, but if employees feel like they can't take advantage of it, it's not doing them any good," says Karen Rubin, managing director, North America, of Talking Talent, which provides coaching to firms such as Deutsche Bank, Bank of America and McKinsey & Co. on family leave transitions.

Some firms, such as Credit Suisse are using data analytics to carefully track whether changes to its leave policy have an impact when it comes to retaining and hiring women. Returning from a maternity leave is a turning point that often results in attrition for female employees, human resources leaders say.

To ease workers' qualms about taking long leaves, KKR, Credit Suisse and others are introducing parental-leave coaching to train both parents and managers as to how to handle monthslong leaves.

Company executives and bosses must set the tone by applauding or promoting employees who take leaves, or by taking significant leaves themselves, say work-and-family experts and HR consultants.

Facebook Inc. founder and chief executive Mark Zuckerberg won plaudits after announcing he would take a two-month paternity leave following the birth of his daughter late last year, a long time for a chief executive but less than the four months of paid leave the company offers to all employees.

Yahoo Inc. chief Marissa Mayer said she would take only a  limited time away" following the birth of her twins in December. Her decision has been criticized by some, but she may have had little choice as Yahoo goes through a critical period where it is considering a sale of its core businesses.

Under Ms. Mayer, however, the company expanded paid leave to as much as 16 weeks.

As of now, most companies pumping up parental benefits are in fields such as technology and finance, where the fight for talent is fierce and where women are underrepresented. Less progress is being made in companies with a more diversified workforce.

Only about 13% of workers have access to paid family leave, according to Bureau of Labor Statistics data and the U.S. remains one of few countries not to mandate paid leave for mothers.

Washington, D.C., is weighing one of the most generous family-leave measure in the country, which would require employers to offer 16 weeks of paid leave to care for a newborn or sick family member. The bill, however, faces opposition from some businesses that contend a mandate would be burdensome.

Paid-leave laws are floating around Congress and the issue is a centerpiece of Democratic presidential candidates' platforms. In this election year, however, individual businesses and municipalities are likely to be the ones driving the biggest changes.

The Property Report
Korean Fund Part of Berlin Deal

By Art Patnaude 
388 words
6 January 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright  2016, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Brookfield Property Partners LP has bought another slice of a European capital city, in partnership with an Asian sovereign-wealth fund.

A person familiar with the deal said the Canadian firm has joined with state-backed Korea Investment Corp. to buy 17 buildings on Potsdamer Platz, a major public square in central Berlin, for 1.3 billion euros ($1.4 billion) from Germany-based Savills Fund Management GmbH.

Brookfield said its joint-venture partner was "an Asian sovereign-wealth fund."

Representatives of the company weren't available for further comment.

Brookfield said in November that it had agreed to acquire the properties there in principle for around 1.3 billion euros.

Potsdamer Platz, at the intersection of five of the German capital city's busiest streets, is home to 480 companies, as well as homes, shops, restaurants and cafes.

Brookfield bought a major London business district, Canary Wharf, last March together with Qatar's state-backed investment fund for GBP 2.6 billion ($3.8 billion).

The large deals helped to make Brookfield the biggest buyer of European real estate in 2015, according to data-provider Real Capital Analytics. Including the Berlin deal, the firm has been involved in $9.2 billion worth of transactions in the past 12 months, the data show.

"Potsdamer Platz is one of the world's iconic properties, and we are pleased to be adding it to Brookfield's growing portfolio of world-class assets," Ric Clark, chairman of Brookfield Property Partners, said in a statement.

Low interest rates have made returns on real estate look attractive to investors, compared with bonds and other assets, and commercial-property markets world-wide have heated up as a result in the past year.

Investors broadly expect the demand to continue in 2016, according to a survey by real-estate broker Colliers International.

More than half of the roughly 600 investors surveyed said they intended to increase property holdings this year, while 11% indicated they planned to reduce exposure to the sector.

In Europe, investors' hunt for returns "do not appear to have dissipated as a key driver of global demand," the Colliers report from December said.

TABB: The big news today at MarketTech appears to be that Google is really very interested in financial services, in the institutional capital markets and we re very excited about that.

ELSTON: Absolutely. We are very focused on financial services, one of the small number of verticals we are interested in. We are intensely focused on finance.

TABB: With Google making their interests known, where do you see Google and the Google Cloud platform bringing innovation to the markets? We ve had a lot of conversations about cloud, but where do you see innovation and Google s name coming together and cloud in the capital markets?

But we want to talk about the innovative aspect of it and the approach. How does Google approach this market, this vertical, that would set it in a leadership position?

ELSTON: Well, we want a close relationship with financial companies, we want that sort of connection with people because we don t just want to change where people do their compute, we want to change how they do their compute. We want people to do differently, to think about analysis differently, to think about the work loads that they are running like risk analytics and simulation, and those sorts of things, we want to change the way people do that, and a lot of the products that we re bringing out in Google cloud platform, like Kubernetes is a way of orchestrating workloads so you don t have to think about oooh how many machines, how many cores, how much memory, you think about it in a different metrics. You think about the compute that you want, how quickly you want to get it done, a small number of ways of classifying this compute and then you do it.

TABB: When we talk about cloud in the financial services one of the topics which always comes up is the question of security. There s a perception that the public cloud is not secure. Yet the data out there shows that the cloud is equally as secure or insecure as institutional data centres. How does Google approach the security question when it comes to cloud?

ELSTON: Google sets a high water mark for security as a technology company. We have 500 plus people focused on security, we build our hardware and software stack from the ground up with security in mind, we build our products with security in mind. Google sets a high water mark for security and I think the perception is changing very quickly is that security is no longer a disadvantage of the cloud, it s an active advantage and differentiator for the cloud, particularly in Google s case, we are using things like homomorphic encryption whereby your data is encrypted at rest, your data is encrypted in flight, your data can stay encrypted whilst it s being processed, encrypted with your own keys, so as well as Google having a set of best practices whereby we can manage keys on your behalf or we don t have to manage the keys on your behalf. ELSTON: Middle and back-office I put into one broad bucket and I think those are good use cases for the cloud today.

Eventually, that way, we can create one happy clappy financial data hub for the whole world, with all the banks dependent on our cloud system. FWIW, maximum trust in us means maximum sharing, and that means maximum potential scaling and the biggest baddest financial vertical of all time. You banks will be familiar with this concept because, well, it s the same concept that underpins banking.

If things go to plan, we might one day be able to migrate entire human souls into the cloud or have all economic activity staged up there. And don t worry about security. Look here s us entrusting you with the keys to your own financial cloud kingdom:

And for a more creatively inspired rendition of the interview, there s this:

TABB: God has looked upon the earth and seen it is wicked because of the shoddy financial services and the institutional capital markets still ravaging the system. We re excited about this, tell us more?

ELSTON: Well, we want a close relationship with financial capital, we want that sort of eternal server connection with people because we don t just want to change where people do their worship, we want to change how they serve. We want people to do it differently, to think about eternal data life differently, to think about how their data lives are dealing with things like sin and temptation, and simulation, and those sorts of things, we want to change the way people do that, and a lot of the products that we re bringing out in Google cloud platform, like the "http://www.computerweekly.com/feature/Demystifying-Kubernetes-the-tool-to-manage-Google-scale-workloads-in-the-cloud", your guide and pilot to the eternal after life, is a way of orchestrating workloads so you don t have to think about eughhh how many shepherds you need to guide the flocks &

[Ed - enough already.]

Some perhaps more useful background: Banks, payment companies and financial service companies are amongst a special group of institutions "http://www.computerweekly.com/opinion/Why-banks-are-wary-of-public-clouds" dependence on third parties when it comes to data and processing services for sensitivity/privacy/proprietary reasons; for liability reasons, for trust reasons, regulatory compliance reasons and for mission critical reasons (they can t afford to suffer an outage). Getting banks to commit to the cloud would therefore be major a feat for computing companies because banks don t really want to be another account name at the Google central service.

A few years back there was a lot of scepticism about whether banks would ever adopt cloud services fully. For a flavour of thinking back then see this"https://www.capgemini.com/resource-file-access/resource/pdf/Cloud_Computing_in_Banking.pdf".

Of course, since fintech exploded onto the scene banks have become more open minded about giving shared services a try   notably because in the new regulatory environment, which makes their banking operations much more capital intensive and less profitable, banks can t afford the luxury of in-house systems anymore. They need to find ways to scale their operations and outsourcing computing systems to third parties is the first obvious thing to do. Hence the obsession with fintech solutions which can cut technology costs all round.

While regulators have always "http://www.computerweekly.com/news/2240110502/Financial-services-regulations-holding-banks-back-from-public-cloud-says-UBS-IT-security-chief", the fact that tech companies are making bold marketing claims about how the banks can have their cake and eat it   e.g. scaled up cloud services and full control of the data centres with total security   many are being convinced the risks have now been greatly reduced. Add to that innovations like blockchain, which aim to distribute data amongst multiple entities in a bid to minimise single-point of failure risk, and the regulators become happier still. (Although, surely the more you distribute and replicate the data, the smaller the scaling cost advantage?)

In any case, banks which want to stay profitable and ahead of the game in the new environment only have three choices: Put all their eggs in one reputable basket for maximum cost advantage (but also maximum concentrated trust risk); distribute their eggs across a number of less well known providers whilst keeping a few at home as well (a cautious testing approach, which might disadvantage them in the short run vs entities that go with the first approach); trust in the power of encryption completely and distribute their eggs to totally anonymous third parties with no accountability (maximum quantum computing breaks all encryption codes one day risk).

There is a fourth option of course: operate just as you are doing today   i.e. less profitably but also with less potential unknown unknown systemic risk.

Sovereign-Wealth Funds Agree to Invest $380 Million in Italian Property; Funds picking up Italian real estate as the nation s economy recovers

By Simon Clark and Art Patnaude 
379 words
5 January 2016
16:39
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

LONDON Sovereign-wealth funds are picking up Italian real estate as the nation's economy recovers.

Government investment funds in Azerbaijan, Abu Dhabi and Qatar in the past two weeks have agreed to invest a combined  ¬350 million ($380 million) to buy and develop three separate properties in Milan, according to a person familiar with the transactions.

The Abu Dhabi Investment Authority is buying a 1960s building that will be demolished to make way for a new tower, and the State Oil Fund of the Republic of Azerbaijan is buying a building from the Italian chamber of commerce, the person said. On Dec. 22, the Qatar Investment Authority agreed to buy a property from BNP Paribas SA.

The purchase prices weren't disclosed. The  ¬350 million includes the cost of redeveloping the three sites. All three will be managed by Italian property company Coima SGR, the person said.

Italy is a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/the-trouble-with-sovereign-wealth-funds-1450836278?tesla=y", which invested $2.2 billion there in 2014 less than a fifth of the amount they invested in the U.K. that year, according to Bocconi University in Milan. That may be changing as the Italian economy, the third largest in the eurozone, picks up after a three-year recession. In February 2015, Qatar's sovereign-wealth fund bought the 60% it didn't already own in Milan's  ¬2 billion Porta Nuova finance district.

Real-estate markets boomed last year in Europe, as investors hunted for returns amid low interest rates and sky-high prices in London. Investors spent around $9.7 billion on Italian real estate last year, compared with $7.2 billion in 2014, according to data-firm Real Capital Analytics. Foreign buyers accounted for 90% of investment into Milan in the first nine months of last year, according to broker Knight Frank.

Spokesmen for the Qatar and Abu Dhabi funds declined to comment. A spokesman for the Azerbaijani fund didn't immediately respond to a request for comment.

Markets
Fannie and Freddie Give Birth to New Mortgage Bond; Government hopes to get taxpayers off the hook if another mortgage crisis arrives

By Joe Light 
1,130 words
30 December 2015
00:15
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac are turning to crisis-era tools to reduce their exposure to mortgage losses and spark a new market for financing home buyers.

Beginning in 2016, the two government-controlled housing giants will ramp up sales of a new type of security that will transfer most of the cost of defaults on all but their safest mortgages to private investors.

The securities will be based on the value of a pool of underlying mortgages but only indirectly, making them a derivative similar to those that figured in the financial crisis seven years ago.

The insurance-like products are called Connecticut Avenue Securities by Fannie Mae and Structured Agency Credit Risk by Freddie Mac. Standard-issue bonds from the housing giants protect investors from the risk that home buyers will stop making payments on their loans. With the new securities, however, investors could lose some or all of their principal if the underlying mortgages default.

The effort marks a notable return to financial engineering in housing finance elements of which served useful purposes before bloating in the years leading to the crisis.

The new securities ultimately could help reduce the government's role in mortgages by persuading investors to take on the risk of default. Right now, the U.S. housing market relies almost entirely on guarantees from Fannie, Freddie or other government-backed entities. The companies, along with government agency Ginnie Mae, back most mortgages and issued 96% of all mortgage bonds in the first 10 months of the year, according to trade publication Inside Mortgage Finance.

Banks used to issue hundreds of millions of dollars worth of mortgage bonds that didn't carry Fannie or Freddie's guarantees, but that market dried up after the financial crisis.

Proponents hope the new securities could help restore investors' appetite for mortgage risk. If it works, backers think the securities could become a mainstay of the bond and housing markets over time, perhaps even getting included in the major indexes tracked by bond funds.

 To many people, mortgage credit risk is still a bad word," said Laurie Goodman, director of the Housing Finance Policy Center at the Urban Institute, adding that she isn't optimistic that the market for privately backed mortgage bonds will revive soon.

Fannie and Freddie already have sold about $25 billion of the securities since 2013 to private investors, including money managers such as BlackRock Inc. and Invesco Ltd., hedge funds, real-estate investment trusts and other investors.

Earlier this month, the Federal Housing Finance Agency, which regulates Fannie and Freddie, set a goal for the companies to transfer most of their risk on many of the new mortgages they back to private investors. The guidance covers mortgages of more than 20 years where homeowners make less than a 40% down payment and says the companies should lay off the risk on 90% of the unpaid principal balance.

Fannie and Freddie don't make loans. They buy them from lenders, wrap them into securities and guarantee to make investors whole if the mortgages default.

In practice, that has meant that investors have taken on the risk of losses if interest rates rise but have left the government with the risk if borrowers default. The new securities along with other methods Fannie and Freddie use to lay off risk mean that the government is increasingly transferring that default risk to private investors as well.

The new securities allocate the risk of default to different tranches. The housing giants typically hold the safest tranche and the riskiest tranche. Investors can buy those in the middle based on their risk and return appetites.

For example, in June, Freddie Mac sold $950 million of Stacrs covering $18.4 billion of Freddie-backed loans. If a severe housing downturn emerged in a few years, those mortgages could be expected to suffer a 2% loss, said Moody's Analytics chief economist Mark Zandi. Without the protection conferred by the securities, Freddie would be hit with losses of $368 million. Instead under the terms of the June deal, Freddie would lose only $143 million, while the private investors would lose $225 million, Mr. Zandi said.

In exchange for taking that risk, in that deal investors got yields of between 1.15 percentage points and 7.55 percentage points above a benchmark short-term interest rate.

Some market watchers note that the companies' programs still are nascent and that the market has yet to show how much appetite it has for the new securities. Fannie and Freddie's early deals only protected them from losses beyond a certain level, though the companies have begun to sell  first-loss risk" as well.

 We're going from no place to some place," said Lewis Ranieri, the financier who co-invented the mortgage-backed security.  There's still a question of whether [the securities sales] can be expanded to really provide the goal of making the government the guarantor of last resort."

Among other outstanding questions are at what price investors will buy the securities in times of market stress and how much issuance it will take until the securities easily trade on the open market. Right now, the debt trades infrequently and tends to stay with the investor who first bought it. New capital standards have caused banks to pull back from dealing in all sorts of bonds, cutting off that avenue for trading as well.

Although BlackRock has bought portions of the Fannie and Freddie deals, it has done so on a limited basis, said Kevin Chavers, BlackRock's managing director, on a panel this month hosted by the Securities Industry and Financial Markets Association, a trade group.

BlackRock's  primary concerns are the lack of liquidity in that marketplace and the lack of liquidity is largely driven by the capital requirements that dealers are required to maintain," Mr. Chavers said.

Hedge funds and money managers have made up the bulk of the earliest investors, with REITS, banks and insurance companies participating to a lesser extent.

Over the next year, proponents of the risk-sharing deals hope for the development of some of the features of a more mature market. New York-based Vista Capital Advisors, a financial-products company specializing in the corporate- bond market, hopes to release indexes based on the Fannie and Freddie bonds early next year, said Richard MacWilliams, managing partner at Vista.

Vista hopes to release products later in the year based on the indexes that would allow investors to trade Fannie and Freddie's credit risk more easily, Mr. MacWilliams said.

Rate-Rise Winners: Foreign Banks

By Katy Burne 
853 words
24 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright  2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Some of the biggest beneficiaries of the Federal Reserve's recent interest-rate increase will be foreign banks.

Units of foreign banks this year received nearly half the roughly $6.25 billion in interest the Fed paid banks on the money, called reserves, they park with the Fed, the central bank's data show. Those institutions control just about 15% of all bank assets in the U.S.

The Fed's interest payments to banks are likely to roughly double next year because in mid-December it raised the rate it pays on reserves to 0.50% from 0.25%. The amounts could rise even more in coming years if the central bank continues lifting the rate, called the interest on excess reserves rate, or IOER.

Foreign banks receive a disproportionate share of the interest payments because they own an outsize share of total reserves.

U.S. branches and agencies of foreign banks had $881 billion of reserves as of June 30, according to Fed data, accounting for about 35% of total reserves. Look at the figures away from month-end financial-reporting dates, and the total is even higher: For the week ended Dec. 2, foreign firms had parked closer to $1.2 trillion at the Fed, or 47% of total reserves.

Foreign banks hold relatively more reserves because of a combination of regulations and incentives, analysts said.

U.S.-chartered banks, including those owned by foreign banking companies, pay premiums to the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. -- ranging from 0.05% to 0.35% -- based on their assets, including reserves but minus other capital measures. But U.S. lenders that don't take deposits, and firms incorporated overseas whose U.S. operations don't take deposits, don't have to pay FDIC fees.

Both foreign and domestic banks can borrow money overnight at low short-term rates and park them at the Fed at a higher rate, earning a profit or "spread." But for some foreign firms, the spread can be larger because they often don't have to pay the FDIC fees.

To illustrate: Before the Fed's rate increase, banks paid about 0.13% to borrow overnight in the federal-funds market and earned 0.25% on their reserves -- a difference of 0.12 percentage point. A foreign bank could get a spread of 0.12%. Domestic banks that paid FDIC fees of about 0.07% on average on their insured deposits would be left with a spread of about 0.05%.

After the rate increase, both types of banks could pay about 0.35% to borrow overnight and get 0.50% on their reserves. That is a 0.15% spread for some foreign banks. But domestic banks, after paying the FDIC fees, get a roughly 0.08% spread.

The New York branches of Deutsche Bank AG and Credit Suisse Group AG each had about $40 billion in reserves at the Fed as of June 30, earning about $100 million in interest.

People familiar with the matter say the extra spread for foreign banks may be offset elsewhere. Units of foreign banks that don't take retail deposits often borrow from other banks in wholesale funding markets that can get adverse regulatory treatment, whereas domestic banks can tap into deposits more cheaply, some of these people said.

Until now, the difference in spreads hasn't been a source of controversy. But it could attract attention as the Fed raises interest rates, which will increase its payments to banks.

Raising the IOER rate to 0.50% from 0.25% will increase the Fed's interest payments to banks to about $13 billion annually, assuming reserves stay at the same level, said Karen Petrou of Federal Financial Analytics Inc.

The Fed is subsidizing both U.S. and foreign banks, said Joseph Gagnon, a former Fed economist now at the Peterson Institute for International Economics, and the latter have a proportionately larger advantage. "I'm surprised it hasn't gotten more attention," Mr. Gagnon said.

A Fed spokesman said, "Payment of interest on reserves is a key tool for setting interest rates at levels appropriate for achieving maximum employment and price stability. We treat all banks that operate in the United States the same."

The Fed started paying interest on reserves in 2008. It plans to use the IOER rate as its primary lever for controlling short-term rates, in part because its postcrisis stimulus policies left markets awash in money, rendering its old tools less effective.

Still, some Fed officials have expressed concern about how it looks to have the central bank making big interest payments to banks.

James Bullard, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis, said in an August radio interview that if Congress isn't comfortable with the size of payments to banks, in particular foreign ones, "They should definitely tell us right now, because . . . we would need to change our exit strategy dramatically if we cannot rely on the interest on excess reserves as being a tool of monetary policy."

Business
Gerson Lehrman Gets New Private-Equity Backer; SFW Capital Partners invests $212 million for a stake in the professional learning company

By Laura Cooper 
543 words
21 December 2015
14:53
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Private-equity firm SFW Capital Partners has invested $212 million for a stake in New York-based professional learning company Gerson Lehrman Group Inc. less than a year after the company's prior backer Silver Lake quietly cashed out.

Gerson Lehrman offers its corporate and nonprofit customers phone consultations and meetings with experts in various industries. The company also offers surveys and customized executive education as well as larger team training and the placement of experts and executives in long-term advisory, operational and board roles.

The company was founded in 1998 and in 2007 received a $200 million investment from Silver Lake, which exited earlier this year. It currently has more than 1,000 employees in 22 offices across 12 countries, although the company's new backers see potential to grow further.

 GLG has many growth opportunities in front of it including expansion of its information product offering and geographic expansion in areas such as Asia and Latin America," said SFW Capital Co-Founder and Partner Roger Freeman.

SFW's investment in the company resulted from a long-standing relationship between the private-equity firm, which focuses on providers of analytics tools and other information services businesses, and GLG, said Mr. Freeman.

 GLG was not actively looking for an investment partner. We reached out to them and initiated a dialogue," said Mr. Freeman. He said that the company then ran a very limited sales process before deciding on Rye, N.Y.-based SFW as its new partner.

The new investment follows a period of heightened regulatory scrutiny of expert networks after several high profile insider trading scandals linked to such experts.

One such scandal involved Mathew Martoma, a portfolio manager at SAC Capital affiliate CR Intrinsic Investors, who is currently serving a nine-year prison sentence for illegally trading on confidential information he received in 2008 from neurology professor Sidney Gilman about an Alzheimer's drug in development at that time.

Dr. Gilman was initially introduced to Mr. Martoma through Gerson Lehrman's expert network, The Wall Street Journal reported in 2012. However, Gerson Lehrman was not targeted in the investigation and Dr. Gilman was not charged in the investigation. Mr. Martoma is appealing his sentence.

For SFW, GLG's compliance program was one of the reasons that the firm decided to invest in the business, which Mr. Freeman said sets the company head and shoulders above the competition.

 We took a very close look at the compliance program and it really does set the standard for compliance in professional learning," he said, noting that a team of over 50 people work on the compliance.  Companies across a wide variety of industries seek out GLG and value its compliance program. We think of it as a competitive advantage and one that customers value."

Mr. Freeman and two additional SFW representatives will join GLG's board as a result of the transaction.

Corrections & Amplifications: Gerson Lehrman Group has more than 1,000 employees. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated the number of employees. (Dec. 21)
