Tough Sales Tactics Used at Philidor --- Mail-order company used by Valeant gave staffers instructions for boosting payments

By Jonathan D. Rockoff and Jeanne Whalen 
1,020 words
29 October 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright  2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

A mail-order pharmacy used by Valeant Pharmaceuticals International Inc. had a simple message for staffers charged with getting health insurers to pay up: Don't take no for an answer.

If a health insurer wouldn't work with Philidor Rx Services LLC, the pharmacy instructed staff to try again using the identification number of a partner pharmacy to secure payment. "We have a couple of different 'back door' approaches to receive payment from the insurance company," a Philidor training manual said.

Canada-based Valeant has said that Philidor and its network of pharmacies was "one of our competitive advantages," providing drugs to patients and a source of sales growth. But use of the pharmacy and its affiliates is drawing scrutiny from analysts and investors concerned about the practice's sustainability.

Some analysts say aggressive tactics could backfire, angering payers and slowing sales at a time when Valeant is under pressure to show it can grow organically and not just through acquisitions. At least one payer, Harvard Pilgrim Health Care in New England, has said that it is reviewing its relationship with Philidor.

Industry experts say Philidor's use of different ID numbers is unusual. The pharmacy's identification number "is always supposed to be connected to the actual provider putting in the claim," said John Norton, spokesman for the National Community Pharmacists Association, a trade group.

The training manual for insurance-claim processors at Philidor and other company documents reviewed by The Wall Street Journal indicate the pharmacy went to great lengths to fill prescriptions when insurers balked. Other instructions include tinkering with a drug's price, lowering it until a payer's system accepts the claim, and then raising it again to seek out a plan's maximum allowable price. Other guidance suggests billing for a lower quantity.

"Philidor is the patient's advocate in seeking to ensure that they receive the medication that was prescribed by their doctor at the lowest possible cost to them. This includes following up with insurance companies on behalf of patients," a Philidor spokesman said.

Valeant, which has asked a committee of board members to examine the company's relationship with Philidor and the pharmacy's work, declined to comment.

Questions about Valeant's business practices and its relationship with Philidor, along with federal investigations of its drug pricing and other issues, have weighed on Valeant's stock, which has lost more than half its value since peaking in August. In recent days, a short seller has accused the company of using Philidor in an accounting scheme, and former Philidor employees say Valeant staffers worked directly in the pharmacy's offices, sometimes using fake names.

Valeant said last week it acquired an option to buy Philidor last December for $100 million and has defended its accounting.

The drug maker has said that Philidor doesn't restrict prescriptions to any particular manufacturers, but former Philidor employees say nearly all of the prescriptions the pharmacy filled are for Valeant drugs.

The Philidor documents reviewed by the Journal reference only Valeant drugs by name. PowerPoint slides instructing insurance-claim processors use Carac, a Valeant drug for ultraviolet-ray damage to the skin, as an example. The training manual gives only a "Valeant Product and Program Overview," citing drugs such as Valeant's acne medicine Solodyn and toenail fungus treatment Jublia.

A bottle of 30 of Solodyn tablets can cost up to $1,040.41, according to Truven Health Analytics, which publishes average wholesale prices. An 8-milliliter bottle of Jublia costs $1,075.72.

To control spending on such pricey medicines, health insurers and companies that manage the plans' drug benefits use a variety of tools, such as directing pharmacists to substitute a lower-priced generic made by a different drug company, and asking patients to pay higher copays. Sometimes, health plans reject a claim because a drug costs too much.

Philidor used various means to lower patient copays. The training manual, which has one page dated in July 2014 and another in October 2014, outlined six different ways that Philidor staff could lower patient copays to as little as $35. "They all essentially do the same thing," the manual said, but using them individually helps Philidor "know which insurances pay better than others."

As far as overcoming rejections, the training manual advised submitting a lower quantity for certain drugs to the payer "to see" if the drugs would then get reimbursed.

The training manual discussed "back door" methods to receiving payment from insurance companies in a section on dealing with insurers "that we are not contracted with" and therefore won't pay Philidor for any medicines.

The manual directed insurance-claims processors to first submit claims using Philidor's unique identification number. If that resulted in an error message saying Philidor doesn't have a contract, the processors should use an identification number for another pharmacy, such as "our partner in California, West Wilshire Pharmacy." That pharmacy didn't respond to a request for comment.

In May 2014, California's board of pharmacy denied Philidor's application for a permit to operate as a pharmacy in the state, saying the pharmacy had made "false statements," including some that concealed Philidor's true owners and beneficiaries, according to documents from the pharmacy board. A few months later, a Delaware-registered firm affiliated with two Philidor executives bought a 10% stake in West Wilshire Pharmacy, of Los Angeles. Philidor has appealed the board's decision.

The PowerPoint, which isn't dated, gave step-by-step instructions on what to do when an insurer rejected a prescription over cost. It advised calling the insurer for an override, and also tinkering with the drug cost until the insurer agreed to pay. Specifically, the manual advised dropping the drug's cost in $500 increments "until paid and then increase by $100 to get as close as possible to the max amount allowed by the insurance company."

WSJ.D Technology: Owner of Weather Channel Sells Some Assets to IBM

By Suzanne Vranica and Robert McMillan 
559 words
29 October 2015
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B3
English
(Copyright (c) 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

International Business Machines Corp. on Wednesday agreed to acquire the digital and data assets of Weather Co., owner of the Weather Channel.

Financial terms weren't immediately disclosed, and calls seeking further comment from IBM were not immediately returned. The Wall Street Journal reported Tuesday that the deal could be valued at more than $2 billion, citing a person familiar with the matter.

The deal doesn't include the traditional TV channel business but does include such digital businesses as the website and app, intellectual property, infrastructure and data.

IBM is particularly interested in Weather Co.'s forecasting group, WSI, which houses the technology and weather data that the Weather Co. collects, manipulates and licenses to companies ranging from airlines to utility companies to insurance providers.

Shares of IBM rose 1.3% to $139.69 in morning trading.

Weather Co. is owned by a consortium that includes private-equity firms Bain Capital LLC and Blackstone Group LP, and Comcast Corp.'s NBCUniversal. They bought the company in 2008 for roughly $3.5 billion. Weather Co. Chief Executive David Kenny is expected to join IBM after the deal closes, according to a person familiar with the matter.

Weather Co. had courted various types of potential buyers. More than a year ago, Google Inc. was offered the chance to bid on the Weather Channel's digital assets, but the company wasn't interested, according to a person familiar with the matter.

Weather Co. is best known for the Weather Channel and its friendly meteorologists like Jim Cantore, who people routinely turn to for information during hurricanes and blizzards. But prospects for the TV channel have dimmed in a tough media environment where consumers are cutting the pay-TV cord and slimming down cable packages. That has raised fears that smaller networks like Weather Channel will struggle to grow subscription fees.

People close to Weather Co. say that the company envisions a future for the TV channel tied to "over-the-top" streaming products. Earlier this year, the company struck a deal to be carried on Dish Network Corp.'s Sling TV streaming video service. A sale of Weather Channel is also on the table, one of the people said.

Weather Co. and IBM already have an alliance, struck earlier this year. The idea was to combine Weather Co.'s weather data and forecast information with IBM's cloud computing skills and analytics know-how, so the two could come up with new ways to package and sell weather data and business solutions to different industries. IBM's artificial intelligence service, Watson, is central to that arrangement.

IBM believes that it can enhance the Weather Channel's forecasts to build new products. Retailers, for example, could better manage their supply chain by knowing when sales might slump because of snowstorms.

Over the past year, IBM has worked hard to secure new data sources for its software, forging new relationships with Twitter Inc., Apple Inc., Johnson & Johnson, Medtronic Inc., and the Weather Channel's WSI division, which generates more than 10 billion weather forecasts a day.

In August, IBM said it would buy Merge Health care, a medical imaging company.

---

Ezequiel Minaya contributed to this article.

Business
Kennedy Wilson Europe Buys 9 Office Properties in Italy; London-listed property firm bought nine buildings rented to Italian government ministries from InvestiRE SGR

By Art Patnaude 
414 words
28 October 2015
21:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Kennedy Wilson Europe Real Estate PLC has made its first foray into Italian real estate, buying a portfolio of office buildings for 185.5 million euros ($205.3 million).

The London-listed property firm bought nine buildings rented to the Italian government ministries from Italian group InvestiRE SGR.

Kennedy Wilson Europe, which was launched on the London Stock Exchange in 2014, also owns property and real-estate loans across Ireland, the U.K. and Spain.

The firm has been hunting for deals in Italy since its initial public offering, said Mary Ricks, president and chief executive of Kennedy Wilson Europe. While this is the first acquisition in Italy,  it's not been for a lack of trying," she said.

Commercial real-estate investment in Italy has more than doubled this year from last year, with around  ¬6.9 billion spent in the first three quarters of the year, according to Real Capital Analytics.

Increased deal making in Italy has been in part driven by surging competition for property assets in bigger international property markets like the U.K. and Germany. Demand has sent prices shooting higher, pushing down prospective returns. Yield-hungry investors have been looking further afield.

For Kennedy Wilson, the yield on its latest portfolio is 6.3%. Leases on the properties expire in December 2022.

 We like the yield a lot," Ms. Ricks said.  When I look at the yield on a government (bond) in Italy, with the same duration, the coupon just doesn't compare."

A recently-issued Italian government bond maturing in September 2022 is yielding 1.02%, according to Tradeweb data.

While investment in Italy has been especially busy this year, sentiment has been strong across Europe. Real estate transaction volumes on the Continent in the first nine months of the year hit  ¬200.7 billion, up 30% from the same period last year, according to Real Capital Analytics.

The biggest office in the Italy portfolio, at 283,400 square feet, is in Milan and leased to the customs and finance ministries. Other office locations include Rome and Florence.

The deal was completed with cash.

InvestiRE SGR is the management company of the closed-end mutual property investment fund known as Fondo Immobili Pubblici.

Tapan Panchal contributed to this article.

Tech
IBM Buys Weather Company s Digital Assets, Expanding Move into Data Crunching; Venerable company continues its acquisition of data-related ventures

By Robert McMillan 
857 words
28 October 2015
22:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

International Business Machines Inc.'s purchase of Weather Company's digital assets extends IBM's bet that it will profit from a new wave of data-crunching software. The deal also catapults the 104-year-old company into a new line of business: consumer advertising.

The companies didn't disclose the acquisition price. The tie-up, which was announced Wednesday, could be worth more than $2 billion, according to a person familiar with the matter.

IBM will hire Weather Company CEO David Kenny and sales chief Jeremy Steinberg along with 900 other Weather Company employees.

The deal doesn't include Weather Company's traditional TV channel business, but does include such digital businesses as the website and app, intellectual property, infrastructure and data. The TV channel will continue as a separate entity owned by a consortium that includes private-equity firms Bain Capital LLC and Blackstone Group LP, and ComcastCorp. 's NBCUniversal.

Shares of IBM were up 2.2% to $140.83 in late trading.

For IBM, based in Armonk, N.Y., Weather Company's primary attraction is data that can feed Watson, the 100-year-old company's self-learning computing platform.

Weather Company, based in Atlanta, Ga., collects 4 gigabytes of data per second from 40 million mobile phones, 147,000 sensors in weather observing stations, airplanes flying 50,000 flights daily, and an undisclosed number of windshield wipers flapping in Internet-connected smartcars. IBM plans to use that data to improve the Weather Company's modeling technology to unleash a new generation of predictive applications.

The move is part of a huge bet IBM has placed that data-rich services and applications delivered over the Internet will make up for shrinking revenue from hardware and information-technology services. IBM has posted revenue declines for 14 consecutive quarters. It is looking for growth from investments in analytics, security, and cloud computing to eventually reverse the decline.

Weather Company's data is the latest addition to IBM's growing stock of information resources. Over the past year, the company has secured new data sources, forging relationships with Twitter Inc., Apple Inc., Johnson & Johnson, and Medtronic Inc. In August, IBM said it would spend $700 million to buy Merge Health care, a medical imaging technology company that came with a trove of 30 billion X-rays, computerized tomography, and magnetic-resonance-imaging scans.

These moves included an earlier partnership with Weather Channel's WSI division, which generates more than 10 billion weather forecasts a day.

In the deal, IBM will also get Weather Company's digital advertising operations, which serve ads to 40 million smartphones every day, reaching a total of 82 million unique monthly visitors, when combined with the Weather.com website.

IBM already sells interactive marketing services to retailers and other businesses. With Weather Company, IBM will have another product to sell to those customers:  a well-placed and well-timed ad," said Bob Picciano, IBM's senior vice president of analytics.

 We think there's a great opportunity for people in retail, but also people who are managing events, people who work in public safety and public sector, [and] financial institutions," Mr. Picciano said.

The WSI forecasting group with its sophisticated weather forecasting software is an valuable asset, said Jon Nese, a meteorology professor at Pennsylvania State University who formerly worked at the Weather Channel. Mr. Nese said that graduates from his program were increasingly being snatched up for corporate tasks such as helping to predict cocoa yields for candy companies or assisting traders on commodity exchanges.

 American business is very quickly realizing that weather impacts the bottom line," he said.  Meteorologists are lurking in the shadows at many, many, many businesses that you would have never thought of before."

The relationship between IBM and Weather Company began as a partnership earlier this year, and it soon became clear that IBM's Watson artificial intelligence software could help the Weather Company's nascent business products reach more customers and deliver greater value.

Mr. Kenny, the Weather Company CEO, said that the partnership had helped the combined companies move beyond weather predictions to actionable advice.  It was actually beginning to find the answers to what people should do,  said Mr. Kenny. "The biggest problem we have with weather forecasts is that people don't always know what to do."

The two companies plan to build new services based on not just weather data, but also other sources such as the sentiment of Twitter users, commerce data from IBM's back-end systems, and personal health data.

 Everything going on in a person is also a big complex system with a lot of interrelated data," said Mr. Kenny.  If we could put all that data in a platform like this, you could better predict what might happen and perhaps be preventative."

Suzanne Vranica contributed to this article

FRONT PAGE - COMPANIES & MARKETS
ICE to buy IDC for $5.2bn as exchange groups snap up rare industry assets

By Philip Stafford 
362 words
27 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
13
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Intercontinental Exchange, the US exchanges operator, is making an aggressive push into fixed-income markets long-dominated by investment banks with the purchase of Interactive Data Corporation in a $5.2bn deal.

Atlanta-based ICE has agreed to pay Silver Lake and Warburg Pincus, IDC's private equity owners, $3.65bn in cash and $1.55bn in common stock for the group, which provides analytics and data for banks and hedge funds for hard-to-value off-exchange instruments.

The deal will throw ICE, best known as a commodity futures exchange operator and owner of the New York Stock Exchange, deep into the market for pricing illiquid bonds. New global banking and markets regulation is set to usher in more electronic quote-driven trading and clearing in fixed-income markets in the US and Europe.

IDC evaluates prices for 2.7m financial securities every day and competes with companies such as Bloomberg.

The deal reflects a growing interest among exchanges in purchasing rare industry assets. Last year, the London Stock Exchange bought Frank Russell, the US stock index provider, for $2.7bn to expand into the US and the exchange traded funds markets, while Deutsche Brse bought the 50 per cent of Stoxx it did not own for $697m.

ICE has been pushing into the market with ventures such as the administration of Libor and IsdaFix and the purchase of UK-based SuperDerivatives for $350m last year. Its market data service revenues have risen from about $100m in 2010 to an expected $700m this year - before the IDC deal was announced - according to UBS. Christopher Harris, analyst at Wells Fargo, said the deal would mean 40 per cent of ICE's group revenues would come from market data, up from just over a fifth.

Silver Lake and Warburg bought IDC in 2010 in a buyout that gave the company an enterprise value of $3.4bn after Pearson, owner of the Financial Times, sold them its 61 per cent stake for $2bn.

ICE Makes Big Bet on Bond Data --- New York Stock Exchange owner to acquire Interactive Data in $5.2 billion deal

By Sarah Krouse and Bradley Hope 
821 words
27 October 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright  2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The owner of the New York Stock Exchange is betting billions that corporate-bond trading is moving into the modern era.

Intercontinental Exchange Inc. said Monday it agreed to buy financial-data provider Interactive Data Corp. from private-equity firms Silver Lake and Warburg Pincus LLC for $5.2 billion. ICE outbid several other suitors, reflecting its view that there is big money to be made from bond-price data as the buying and selling of corporate debt shifts to electronic marketplaces.

Interactive Data competes with companies including Bloomberg LP and Thomson Reuters Corp. in providing pricing and data services to traders, portfolio managers and risk and compliance teams at financial institutions. Among its best-known products is hard-to-get pricing data on corporate bonds.

While stocks are bought and sold on exchanges, bonds traditionally have been traded over the phone between large banks and brokers. Over the past two decades several brokerage, financial-technology and other firms have launched venues for electronic bond trading that they say offer more transparency about prices and an easier way for buyers and sellers to connect. Those efforts have helped move a small but growing share of transactions to the new marketplaces.

Kevin McPartland, head of market-structure research at consulting firm Greenwich Associates, estimates that electronic bond trading could represent as much as 30% of overall trading in the next few years, up from about 20% today.

Analysts said that by acquiring Interactive Data, ICE is in a position to profit in two ways: by launching its own corporate bond-trading platform and selling its data to other emerging trading venues, in addition to the firm's current roster of banking, asset management and other clients.

"The data is valuable, but the company is suggesting it opens them up to other things as well," said Richard Repetto, an analyst at Sandler O'Neill + Partners.

Scott Hill, ICE's finance chief, said at an investor conference in September that the firm is in the "early days" of expanding in the data business. A person familiar with the matter said the main driver of the deal for ICE was the stable revenue from Interactive Data's data and analytics, though the firm hasn't ruled out launching a trading platform.

Some investors are skeptical that corporate bonds will be traded on exchanges to the same degree as stocks, citing the relative complexity of bonds.

"You're a long way off from seeing fixed-income trade on an exchange," said Scott Kimball, senior portfolio manager at Taplin, Canida & Habacht LLC, which is part of BMO Financial Group. "Fixed income is just not a one-size-fits-all marketplace."

The deal will make ICE the third-largest financial market data provider behind Bloomberg and Thomson Reuters, according to Burton-Taylor International Consulting LLC, which tracks the firms' market share. Exchanges have increasingly sought to diversify their businesses, with some focusing on data provision as a source of steady revenue.

The data provider attracted heavy interest because it has bond pricing that isn't available elsewhere, people familiar with the discussions said. Talks around an acquisition accelerated in recent weeks after the firm filed paperwork for a potential initial public offering, the people said. Terms of the ICE deal were completed late Sunday night, they said.

The other bidders at the late stage were Markit Ltd., which competes with Interactive Data to provide banks and investors with bond-pricing data, and Nasdaq Inc., which is hoping to expand its fixed-income businesses, the people said. ICE ultimately topped their bids by several hundred million dollars, the people said.

Banks, asset managers and global organizations have raised concerns in recent years about corporate-bond-market liquidity, or difficulty buying and selling bonds without significantly affecting prices. Some argue that postcrisis regulations that make it more expensive for banks to hold large inventories of bonds have hurt market liquidity.

Trading-platform providers say they help facilitate trades by connecting buyers and sellers and shedding more light on pricing. The International Monetary Fund said in an October report on financial stability that electronic trading platforms could boost trading volumes and market liquidity in over-the-counter markets.

ICE, which bought the New York Stock Exchange in 2013, is paying $3.65 billion in cash and $1.55 billion in shares for Interactive Data. The deal is expected to close by year-end.

Silver Lake and Warburg Pincus stand to nearly triple their money on their 2010 acquisition of Interactive Data, reflecting the recent surge in demand for fixed-income data. The value of their roughly $1.2 billion stake in the original deal is worth over $3 billion in the sale to ICE.

J.P. Morgan to Retool Its Chase Pay Product

By Emily Glazer and Robin Sidel 
460 words
27 October 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright  2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. is overhauling its payment product in a way that will allow it to compete more directly with the likes of Apple Inc. and PayPal Holdings Inc.

The New York-based bank is retooling its Chase Pay product next year to make it easier for its 94 million credit, debit and prepaid cardholders to buy goods and services through retailers' websites, mobile apps and physical stores.

The move is the latest by a big bank motivated by the perceived gold rush that financial and technology companies are racing toward as more consumers buy things on their mobile phones. Financial firms are also rushing to serve consumers who may bypass credit cards in the future.

The nation's largest bank by assets on Monday said it is partnering with some of the country's largest retailers starting in mid-2016, including Wal-Mart Stores Inc., Target Corp. and Best Buy Co.

The bank's executives hope the companies will push their customers to pay with the new Chase Pay apps since that may also save money for the merchants.

With the changes, J.P. Morgan Chase customers will see the Chase logo pop up on merchants' apps or their sites. Currently, customers shopping online wouldn't see the logo but might encounter gateways to pay through Apple Pay or PayPal.

Even as more consumers bank online and check their accounts with mobile phones, many have yet to fully adopt digital or mobile payments, whether using a technology or a financial firm, industry experts say. But J.P. Morgan Chase believes it has an edge given its large customer base and merchant research.

"We've got the scale to bring an awful lot of customers," said Gordon Smith, the head of J.P. Morgan's consumer and community banking, in an interview.

Banks have put a lot of investment into digital and mobile wallets in recent years, but consumers have often preferred to swipe the old-fashioned way. A survey released by the research firm Gallup in July found that 13% of U.S. adult smartphone owners have a digital wallet app but around three out of every four of them rarely, if ever, use it.

"The reality is no one knows which [digital wallet] will win yet," said Mr. Smith, who introduced the changes at the Money20/20 financial-technology conference in Las Vegas.

The bank announced a broad initiative around Chase Pay two years ago and has spent the past nine months building the technology in-house, with an initial 250 employees across technology, design, business, analytics and research, Mr. Smith said.

Citi Tests ATMs That Scan Your Eyes

By Peter Rudegeair 
725 words
27 October 2015
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B9
English
Copyright  2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Will ATM machines soon be able to identify you by your eyes?

Citigroup Inc. is testing new technology with automated-teller-machine maker Diebold Inc. that would allow customers to withdraw money with an eyeball scan or a code on a smartphone instead of a card swipe.

The new technology announced by Diebold on Monday, is the latest foray by big banks to find easier, more secure ways for consumers to access their cash than the ATM card, a staple in consumers' wallets for decades.

Citigroup may decide not to retool its machines with this new wrinkle, and any mass rollout may be years away. Other big banks including J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. and Bank of America Corp. recently started internally testing their own cardless ATM technologies.

Citigroup's experiment involves a new kind of cash machine that lacks a screen or touchpad. Instead, customers would first check the bank's mobile app on their smartphone or tablet ahead of time to sign in and select how much money they want to withdraw. Then, they would approach the machine, which would quickly scan their iris to verify their identity. The machine, which connects to the mobile app, would spit out the right amount of cash.

J.P. Morgan showed off a cardless ATM during its investor day earlier this year but hasn't yet offered consumers a demonstration. A bank spokeswoman said it is also evaluating the use of voice- and facial-recognition technology. A Bank of America spokeswoman said the bank has tested cardless ATMs internally and will begin a customer pilot of them during the first half of next year. Similar features have already been rolled out at the ATMs of smaller banks, such as BMO Harris Bank, a Chicago-based unit of Canada's Bank of Montreal, and Rosemont, Ill.-based Wintrust Financial Corp.

Now, interest from some of the largest U.S. banks signals that more consumers may see the option. "Larger players sometimes like to wait on the sidelines a little longer to see if a product has merit before investing in it," said Daniel Van Dyke, an analyst at Javelin Strategy & Research.

"Everyone is doing more and more on their mobile device," said Wayne Malone, Citigroup's head of global ATMs, in an interview. Between 2013 and 2015, the share of bank customers who said their first preference for basic banking was using their mobile phone rose to 13% from 5%, according to Javelin.

It remains to be seen whether consumers who are generally suspicious of large financial institutions would be comfortable letting a bank scan their eyeballs regularly.

Changing thousands of ATMs' hardware to make use of some of these features would probably be expensive and time-consuming. That means it would likely be tested extensively before any full rollout.

Proponents say the new technologies may give banks a weapon in the fight against frauds that target ATM transactions. Credit-scoring and analytics firm FICO said in May that the number of attacks on consumers' debit cards used at ATMs in roughly the first three months of the year hit the highest level in at least 20 years.

The new technology, by contrast, wouldn't need a card, which means a card's information couldn't be skimmed by machines that are attached to ATMs. The new machines also don't have PIN pads, which thieves have spied on using tiny cameras.

BMO Harris currently has more than 900 ATMs running the software that allows cash withdrawals without bank cards, said Doug Peacock, the bank's lead executive for mobile banking. Mr. Peacock declined to give the number of customers using the cardless option but said it was increasing by a double-digit annualized basis each month and that growth has so far exceeded its goals.

Cardless transactions take about 15 seconds to complete compared with around 45 seconds for more traditional transactions, Mr. Peacock added.

Diebold said cash withdrawals on the ATMs developed with Citigroup can be completed in less than 10 seconds. Citigroup tested the new technology in August with about 30 consumers in a lab it keeps on the 10th floor of its skyscraper in the Long Island City neighborhood of Queens, N.Y.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
 Big data  killed the wealth management star; COMMENT

By Yuri Bender 
623 words
26 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
08
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

More than 35 years ago, The Buggles, a British band, recorded "Video killed the radio star". The song heralded the onset of an optimistic era of automation, while lamenting some of its side-effects.

Today, in the new-age technology hubs serving the financial centres of London, Paris and Amsterdam, those concerns are gaining ground.

In the twin towers of Socit Gnrale, sprouting out of the purpose-built Parisian business district of La Dfense, private-banking boss Jean-Francois Mazaud describes his "test and learn" approach that was kick-started in the bank's "innovation lab" five years ago.

Idealistic graduates from French business schools, who once would have been busy constructing ill-fated derivative-based structured products, are turning their hand to financial technology, apparently "part of the group's DNA".

The old Buggles nightmare of a system that "can't rewind" is played down by Mr Mazaud, in favour of equilibrium between man and machine. A human-based wealth management offer should allow clients to set their own cursor to regulate time spent with their adviser.

The issues jangling the nerves of French financiers are shared by the pragmatic bankers of the bleak Dutch hinterland in Amsterdam-Zuid, home to ABN Amro, the country's leading wealth manager.

All of them have visited Asia and seen how far banks there are ahead of the technology curve. Most foreign competitors are watching DBS , one of Singapore's leading providers, closely. It is introducing a predictive system from IBM, the technology company, allowing staff to monitor vast reams of data and assist asset-allocation decisions for clients.

This has been influenced, in turn, both by tech-savvy Korean banks and Chinese groups such as Alibaba , the ecommerce group, and Tencent , the gaming and messaging company.

The question for private bankers is how far they can afford to go into this battle with data? Publicly most will say they will do whatever it takes, even if they have private reservations. " Big data are not just a big dream," says Jeroen Rijpkema, chief executive of international private banking at ABN Amro.

This obsession with data delivery and robotic channels of communication has its dangers. Indeed, many of the existing private banking problems of poor asset allocation and the sale of incorrect products are not really solved, but exacerbated.

Blind digitisation will not help heal those wounds. "The only way you can improve this is by changing the underlying model and processes," says Tim Tate, global head of client experience at Citi Private Bank's Canary Wharf headquarters in London.

Much of his bank's transformation process is influenced by its Citi Ventures private equity arm in Palo Alto, California, which helps Citi take a five-year view of digitisation and not get bogged down in the daily IT grind of updating websites and tweaking apps.

Digitisation has, so far, improved the "line speed" of existing services, but it has yet to cause any real disruption or innovation, says Amin Rajan, chief executive of Create Research, an asset management consultancy.

Unlike retailers and other commerce sectors, wealth and asset managers are still missing a huge digital opportunity. "The mindset of many firms is simply to adapt existing capabilities," says Sebastian Dovey, co-founder of Scorpio Partnership, the wealth management consultancy.

The next stage has to be a complete transformation of the industry, and, believes Mr Dovey, revising the engagement rules between bank, client and wealth. They could even go as far as aiming for the sentiment of another hit song of the Buggles era, Fern Kinney's "Together we are beautiful".

Yuri Bender is editor-in-chief of Professional Wealth Management

Ex-NSA Chief s Cybersecurity Startup Draws Funding; Infusion of $32.5 million in IronNet highlights ties between Silicon Valley and Washington

By Danny Yadron 
567 words
26 October 2015
00:29
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The former head of the National Security Agency has attracted funding for his cybersecurity startup from a prominent venture-capital firm, highlighting the continuing ties between Silicon Valley and Washington despite recent tensions.

Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers is among the investors providing a $32.5 million infusion to IronNet Cybersecurity Inc., which aims to help companies fight computer hackers with software.

IronNet was founded by Keith Alexander, who was NSA director in 2013 when former agency contractor Edward Snowden revealed that the NSA had snooped with help from technology firms. Since then, government officials and tech executives have clashed over the proper limits of encryption technology.

The IronNet investment was led by Donald Dixon, an old friend of Mr. Alexander, at Trident Capital Cybersecurity, another West Coast investment firm.

The transition from the NSA to the cybersecurity industry has become fairly common in an age of data breaches blamed on foreign hackers. Mr. Alexander's three most recent predecessors went on to work at Washington-area consulting and investment firms.

Mr. Alexander, a retired four-star four-star general, has taken a more tech-oriented route with IronNet, teaming up with a University of Chicago professor to build antihacking software. A bit of a geek, Mr. Alexander was known for modeling an NSA command center after the interior of Star Trek's USS Enterprise.

IronNet is the latest of several security companies seeking to profile hacker behavior and create computer models to spot it, so they can detect threats more quickly. That could put Mr. Alexander in competition with companies such as FireEye Inc., CrowdStrike Inc. or Palantir Technologies Inc., the data-analytics company that reportedly helped U.S. spies find Osama bin Laden.

In an interview, Mr. Alexander declined to discuss IronNet's progress. He said IronNet has several paying customers, but wouldn't disclose names.

 We don't want them to know how far we've gone," he said, referring to potential competitors. On its website, IronNet uses a mix of tech and military language, promising  real-time visibility and situational awareness across the information environment."

IronNet was launched shortly after Mr. Alexander left the NSA in spring 2014. Many of its employees are drawn from the federal government, including Matthew Olsen, who led counterterrorism efforts for the Obama administration.

As IronNet builds its technology, officials such as Mr. Olsen have offered consulting services to bring in extra revenue. Mr. Alexander said he has been boning up on how to run a startup, such as drafting term sheets for investors.

 It's been a good year," Mr. Alexander said of IronNet in an interview.  I'm not going to say it's been an easy year."

It also hasn't been without controversy. Some critics accused Mr. Alexander of too quickly cashing in or potentially relying on classified intelligence to turn a buck.

This spring, the former general asked the NSA to review IronNet's patent filings for classified material and to check that it didn't borrow from the work of others at the agency. The company doesn't appear to have made any changes since the review.

Mr. Alexander said his patents are submitted and pending.

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - MONEY
Investment trusts dust off their dowdy image as boardrooms are shaken up

By Judith Evans 
2,435 words
24 October 2015
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
10
English
Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The battle for control at Alliance Trust captured headlines this year, but it is not the only fund experiencing radical change, as Judith Evans reports

The insult still stings. Five years ago, Peter Hargreaves, founder of fund supermarket Hargreaves Lansdown, told the FT: "I do not think the investment trust industry is capable of being revived.

It's run by a lot of fuddy duddies who have no place in the industry." The 142-year-old trusts sector responded defensively. One supporter admitted trusts had "as much charisma as a three-day-old haddock", but insisted the sector was "more dynamic than its public persona would suggest". Others produced performance data suggesting the fuddy-duddies were outperforming their open-ended cousins.

In any case, Mr Hargreaves' prediction of doom for the sector has not been fulfilled. In market capitalisation terms, investment trusts have since expanded from 77bn to 112bn - growth of 45 per cent. Ironically, Hargreaves Lansdown itself is now one of the largest investment trust brokers in the country, with 2.9bn held in the products on its platform.

Listed funds have become a fashionable format for new asset classes - renewable energy, peer-to-peer debt - and have drawn several US companies to launch products listed on the London Stock Exchange.

Perhaps most importantly, the Retail Distribution Review in 2013 removed the ability of unlisted mutual funds to pay commissions to financial advisers and other intermediaries, something trusts had never been able to do. That change brought trusts on to a level playing field with unlisted rivals in the retail market.

Trusts' ability to "smooth over" their dividend payments and use borrowing to enhance returns suddenly appealed to a wider audience, while the new, more direct competition with mutual funds has prompted a series of trusts to overhaul themselves.

"There is tremendous demand for trusts now, and loads of discounts [to net asset value] have been wiped out," says John Newlands, head of investment companies at wealth manager Brewin Dolphin.

Modern times

The latest trust to be dragged, more or less kicking and screaming, into the modern era was Alliance Trust, a 127-year-old, 2.7bn global vehicle that is a Dundee institution.

After a long battle with Elliott, a US-based activist shareholder, Alliance Trust this month drew dividing lines between the trust itself and its internal asset management arm, awarding Alliance Trust Investments the mandate to run the trust at a low fee of 35 basis points.

It also set out plans to become purely equity-focused, adopted a fixed benchmark and performance target, and switched to a fully cataindependent non-executive board, while it will try - partly through share buybacks - to reduce its discount to net asset value into the single-digits.

Jeremy Beckwith, director of manager research for the UK at Morningstar, says the changes were "something of a blueprint for best practice in corporate governance, especially at the self-managed trusts".

"Everyone is much clearer about what they're trying to do and who's accountable for delivering it," says Mr Beckwith.

"The board has told Alliance Trust Investments, 'This is your mandate and you're now responsible for going on and achieving that. If you don't, we might fire you'." The governance changes mean now could be a good time to buy Alliance Trust, since they are likely to filter through into performance improvements, says James Maltin, investment director at Rathbone Investment Management. "I don't think one could do a great deal of harm in buying Alliance Trust," he says.

Elliott had argued Alliance was sustaining a bloated management structure while failing to deliver better performance than a cheap tracker fund. To push for changes, it nominated people to the board who it said would be fully independent of the trust's management.

Trust boards are meant to oversee the funds impartially, but genuine independence is a perennial problem for those trusts which incorporate their own management rather than farming out the function to a separate asset manager.

Independence may also be a problem "where asset management companies have a family of investment trusts - the board members are essentially appointed with heavy influence from the underlying management company," says Mr Beckwith.

"Historically, [boards] have been seen as fairly cosy places to be, a nice, gentle way to earn 20,000 or 25,000 a year as a non-exec on an investment trust.

"But the pressure has been building over the past few years from activist investors. Boards should be thinking about what they can do to prevent an activist attack." While Alliance Trust came under intense external pressure, the boards of some trusts have themselves been the cataindependent lysts for change. Directors of the underperforming 117-year-old British Assets Trust switched its fund manager from F&C to BlackRock in February, and won existing investors over to a change of mandate from equities to multi-asset income.

The renamed BlackRock Income Strategies trust is now at the front of efforts by BlackRock, the world's largest asset manager, to cater for UK pensioners in search of a steady income.

It is one of nine investment trusts to have switched management teams since the start of 2014. Another, Monks Investment Trust, changed managers within Baillie Gifford, the firm that runs it, after five years of underperformance; the new manager, Charles Plowden, immediately changed half the fund's holdings.

Overhauls have paid off for some of Britain's biggest trusts. Witan, which dates back more than 100 years, in 2004 adopted a multi-manager approach and ditched Henderson Global Investors, its former managers, in favour of running itself. It also adopted an extreme approach to share buybacks - reducing its number of shares by almost 50 per cent over 15 years.

The result has been improving performance and a narrowing discount to net asset value - so much so that the company is now issuing new shares again.

Bubbles of complacency

Dinosaurs still lurk among the more than 400 London-listed trusts, at least according to one well-known analyst, Alan Brierley at Canaccord Genuity.

"Corporate governance has improved significantly, but I get the impression that a seven-year bull market has created bubbles of comlysts placency out there," says Mr Brierley.

"There are some top-drawer managers, but also some distinctly average companies where managers are unable to deliver sustained outperformance, and some companies that are too small to compete in this marketplace." Some 130 investment trusts have market capitalisations of less than 50m, prompting questions over their future viability.

Mr Brierley notes that it is impossible to set a minimum level of assets for a viable trust. Some, such as those focused on small- or microcaps, may need to limit growth to ensure they carry out their investment strategies.

But he adds: "For traditional longonly equity funds with a focus on large- and mid-caps, questions have to be asked if the managers are not able to grow the market cap beyond 100m." Meanwhile, more than half of trusts still have performance fees in place - additional charges on top of administration fees, which Mr Beckwith says may be justified in specialist funds, but are hard to defend in mainstream sectors such as UK or global equities. Some 20 funds have ditched them since the start of 2014.

Other funds face question marks over their treatment of investors. One trust, the 227m JZ Capital Partners, won the label "a fund to avoid" from analysts at Numis last month after it issued 79m of equity, diluting the holdings of existing shareholders, despite trading at a steep discount of 40 per cent.

Among global trusts, one of the worst performing over five years is the 631m British Empire Securities and General Trust, run by Asset Value Investors. In share price terms it has returned only 9.3 per cent to investors over five years, in contrast with 159 per cent for its most successful rival, the Lindsell Train Investment Trust, according to figures from FE Analytics.

That resulted in part from market conditions unsuited to the British Empire trust's "value" style, and its mandate to invest in stocks from any country except the US, where markets have been buoyant over the past seven years.

The trust is undergoing a quiet revolution, however. A new manager, Joe Bauernfreund, took charge last month and admits performance has been "less than impressive". He plans to reduce its 40 holdings to nearer 30.

Stops and starts

Radicalism does not always work.

Two other funds, LMS Capital and Dexion Absolute, sought to take on new investment mandates in the summer, only to see their plans knocked back by shareholders.

In parallel with Alliance Trust's hedge fund battle, Electra - a 1.7bn private equity trust - has been under attack from another activist, Edward Bramson of Sherborne Investors Management.

For a second time, Mr Bramson, who now owns almost 30 per cent of Electra, has called a vote on November 5 on whether he and Ian Brindle, a former chair of Sherborne, should join Electra's board. A year ago, Mr Bramson was defeated when shareholders voted down a similar proposal.

Sherborne boasts of having made its investors an average of 19 per cent a year, after fees, for the 12 years to the end of 2014. The performance of its target, Electra, has not been so consistent - but in the past year, according to FE Analytics data, Electra has made its investors 45 per cent.

This has led analysts and shareholders to question whether Electra is really in need of a rude awakening.

But there are parallels between Mr Bramson's arguments and those which triumphed at Alliance Trust: he told shareholders in a letter on October 16 that costs were too high and the board was not independent enough from Electra Partners, which manages the investments.

Electra has made some changes since Mr Bramson began buying the trust, introducing a dividend policy and cutting costs. But Electra's management shot back on October 21: "It is deeply disappointing that Mr Bramson has shown such wilful ignorance concerning the success of Electra and how we operate. He has made a series of ill-judged, ill-informed and ill-founded claims about the company and its board - we cannot let this stand." Analysts at Numis believe the management may now lose the vote, since Mr Bramson has not only increased his stake but won support from some institutional investors. But Charles Cade, head of investment company research, adds: "Whichever way the vote goes, the long term future of the fund appears uncertain, in our view, due to the divided nature of the shareholder base."

Back to the future

Investment trusts have many qualities that open-ended funds will never have. Their capacity is limited by the number of shares in issue, so unlike mutual funds they cannot carry on gathering assets - and collecting percentage- based fees - indefinitely.

They can accommodate illiquid assets such as property without worrying so much about how to sell them if investors desert the fund en masse.

But the listed structure comes with its own constraints, which have kept investment trusts a minority sport even as they become better known.

Liquidity in trusts themselves can be a concern, and big wealth managers and adviser groups can shy away from recommending them because it may not be feasible for large numbers of clients to own the same fund.

Mr Newlands is a long-term fan of trusts but says: "Trusts under 200m are harder and harder to recommend, because investors need the reassurance they can get out as easily as they got into the position.

"We are increasingly aware of the importance of good liquidity and we wish to avoid concentration risk." Mr Brierley argues the sector is in need of more mergers, but questions whether this will happen given that boards which opt to merge are effectively putting themselves out of a job - "six plus six does not equal 12[on a board]".

Analysts hope that more direct competition with open-ended funds, especially now that investors can easily compare performance online, will keep up the pressure for lacklustre trusts to reform.

But the sector is also in a sweet spot as demand has increased, pushing share prices up and discounts to net asset value down.

"We all love to buy high-quality vehicles on high discounts, but there are not too many of those now," says Mr Brierley. "You're having to work a lot harder now than back in 2009."

Discounts

Are they bargains - or cheap for a reason?

When an investment trust trades at a steep discount to net asset value, potential investors face a dilemma. If they buy, will they get a bargain or does the discount reflect poor prospects for the fund, perhaps because of a flawed investment strategy or an asset class under stress? BlackRock's World Mining trust is a case in point. It suffered badly from the downturn in the commodities and resources sectors, and took a steep writedown a year ago after the failure of one of its largest holdings, London Mining, a Sierra Leone iron ore miner. As a result, its share price has fallen almost 34 per cent in the past year and it trades at an 11 per cent discount.

But the fund made a series of changes in March, including a new fee structure, and it may now be time to reconsider the trust, according to analysts at Winterflood.

"We believe that the fund remains an interesting play on a recovery in the resources sector," they say.

Canaccord Genuity recommends a series of "opportunities for the contrarian", including Templeton Emerging Markets, an underperforming trust that also trades at an 11 per cent discount.

John Newlands, head of investment companies research at the wealth manager Brewin Dolphin, argues there may be opportunities in the private equity sector. He suggests looking at products such as Pantheon International Participations, a fund of funds trading at a 15 per cent discount.

"Private equity is a slightly damaged brand - a number of them suffered quite badly in the credit crunch when they were overcommitted and had to sell holdings almost at fire sale prices," says Mr Newlands. "But they've learned their lesson and it's one of the few sectors where there are some attractive discounts left." Buyers must tread carefully, however. A big discount means the market is dubious, and investors should not take the plunge without understanding why.

Investment trusts dust off their dowdy image

Judith Evans

23 October 2015

Copyright 2015 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The insult still stings. Five years ago, Peter Hargreaves, founder of the fund supermarket Hargreaves Lansdown, told the Financial Times:  I do not think the "http://www.ft.com/personal-finance/investments" trust industry is capable of being revived. It s run by a lot of fuddy duddies who have no place in the industry. 

The 142-year-old trusts sector responded defensively. One supporter admitted trusts had  as much charisma as a three-day-old haddock , but insisted the sector was  more dynamic than its public persona would suggest . Others produced performance data suggesting that the fuddy-duddies were in fact outperforming their open-ended cousins.

In any case, Mr Hargreaves  prediction of doom for the sector has not been fulfilled. In market capitalisation terms, investment trusts have since expanded from 77bn to 112bn   growth of 45 per cent. Ironically, Hargreaves Lansdown itself is now one of the largest investment trust brokers in the country, with 2.9bn held in the products on its platform.

Listed funds have become a fashionable format for new asset classes   renewable energy assets, peer-to-peer debt   and have drawn several US companies to launch products listed on the London Stock Exchange.

Perhaps most importantly, the Retail Distribution Review in 2013 removed the ability of unlisted mutual funds to pay commissions to financial advisers and other intermediaries, something trusts had never been able to do. That change brought trusts on to a level playing field with their unlisted rivals in the retail market.

Trusts  ability to  smooth over  their dividend payments and use borrowing to enhance returns suddenly appealed to a wider audience, while the new, more direct competition with mutual funds has prompted a series of trusts to overhaul themselves.

 There is tremendous demand for trusts now, and loads of discounts [to net asset value] have been wiped out,  says John Newlands, head of investment companies at wealth manager Brewin Dolphin.

Modern times

The latest trust to be dragged, more or less kicking and screaming, into the modern era was Alliance Trust, a 127-year-old, 2.7bn global vehicle that is a Dundee institution.

After a long battle with Elliott, a US-based activist shareholder, Alliance Trust this month drew dividing lines between the trust itself and its internal asset management arm, awarding Alliance Trust Investments the mandate to run the trust at a low fee of 35 basis points.

It also set out plans to become purely equity-focused, adopted a fixed benchmark and performance target, and switched to a fully independent non-executive board, while it will try   partly through share buybacks   to reduce its discount to net asset value into the single-digits.

Jeremy Beckwith, director of manager research for the UK at Morningstar, says the changes were  something of a blueprint for best practice in corporate governance, especially at the self-managed trusts .

 Everyone is much clearer about what they re trying to do and who s accountable for delivering it,  says Mr Beckwith.

 The board has told Alliance Trust Investments,  This is your mandate and you re now responsible for going on and achieving that. If you don t, we might fire you . 

The governance changes mean now could be a good time to buy Alliance Trust, since they are likely to filter through into performance improvements, says James Maltin, investment director at Rathbone Investment Management.  I don t think one could do a great deal of harm in buying Alliance Trust,  he says.

Elliott had argued Alliance was sustaining a bloated management structure while failing to deliver better performance than a cheap tracker fund. To push for changes, it nominated people to the board who it said would be fully independent of the trust s management.

Trust boards are meant to oversee the funds impartially, but genuine independence is a perennial problem for those trusts which incorporate their own management rather than farming out the function to a separate asset manager.

Independence may also be a problem  where asset management companies have a family of investment trusts   the board members are essentially appointed with heavy influence from the underlying management company,  says Mr Beckwith.

 Historically, [boards] have been seen as fairly cosy places to be, a nice, gentle way to earn 20,000 or 25,000 a year as a non-exec on an investment trust.

 But the pressure has been building over the past few years from activist investors. Boards should be thinking about what they can do to prevent an activist attack. 

While Alliance Trust came under intense external pressure, the boards of some trusts have themselves been the catalysts for change. Directors of the underperforming 117-year-old British Assets Trust switched its fund manager from F&C to BlackRock in February, and won existing investors over to a change of mandate from equities to multi-asset income.

The renamed BlackRock Income Strategies trust is now at the front of efforts by BlackRock, the world s largest asset manager, to cater for UK pensioners in search of a steady income.

It is one of nine investment trusts to have switched management teams since the start of 2014. Another, Monks Investment Trust, changed managers within Baillie Gifford, the firm that runs it, after five years of underperformance; the new manager, Charles Plowden, immediately changed half the fund s holdings.

Overhauls have paid off for some of Britain s biggest trusts. Witan, which dates back more than 100 years, in 2004 adopted a multi-manager approach and ditched Henderson Global Investors, its former managers, in favour of running itself. It also adopted an extreme approach to share buybacks   reducing its number of shares by almost 50 per cent over 15 years.

The result has been improving performance and a narrowing discount to net asset value   so much so that the company is now issuing new shares again.

Bubbles of complacency

Dinosaurs still lurk among the more than 400 London-listed trusts, at least according to one well-known analyst, Alan Brierley at Canaccord Genuity.

 Corporate governance has improved significantly, but I get the impression that a seven-year bull market has created bubbles of complacency out there,  says Mr Brierley.

 There are some top-drawer managers, but also some distinctly average companies where managers are unable to deliver sustained outperformance, and some companies that are too small to compete in this marketplace. 

Some 130 investment trusts have market capitalisations of less than 50m, prompting questions over their future viability.

Mr Brierley notes that it is impossible to set a minimum level of assets for a viable trust. Some, such as those focused on small- or microcaps, may need to limit growth to ensure they carry out their investment strategies.

But he adds:  For traditional long-only equity funds with a focus on large- and mid-caps, questions have to be asked if the managers are not able to grow the market cap beyond 100m. 

Meanwhile, more than half of trusts still have performance fees in place   additional charges on top of administration fees, which Mr Beckwith says may be justified in specialist funds, but are hard to defend in mainstream sectors such as UK or global equities. Some 20 funds have ditched them since the start of 2014.

Other funds face question marks over their treatment of investors. One trust, the 227m JZ Capital Partners, won the label  a fund to avoid  from analysts at Numis last month after it issued 79m of equity, diluting the holdings of existing shareholders, despite trading at a steep discount of 40 per cent.

Among global trusts, one of the worst performing over five years is the 631m British Empire Securities and General Trust, run by Asset Value Investors. In share price terms it has returned only 9.3 per cent to investors over five years, in contrast with 159 per cent for its most successful rival, the Lindsell Train Investment Trust, according to figures from FE Analytics.

That resulted in part from market conditions unsuited to the British Empire trust s  value  style, and its mandate to invest in stocks from any country except the US, where markets have been buoyant over the past seven years.

The trust is undergoing a quiet revolution, however. A new manager, Joe Bauernfreund, took charge last month and admits performance has been  less than impressive . He plans to reduce its 40 holdings to nearer 30.

Stops and starts

Radicalism does not always work. Two other funds, LMS Capital and Dexion Absolute, sought to take on new investment mandates in the summer, only to see their plans knocked back by shareholders.

In parallel with Alliance Trust s hedge fund battle, Electra   a 1.7bn private equity trust   has been under attack from another activist, Edward Bramson of Sherborne Investors Management.

For a second time, Mr Bramson, who now owns almost 30 per cent of Electra, has called a vote on November 5 on whether he and Ian Brindle, a former chair of Sherborne, should join Electra s board. A year ago, Mr Bramson was defeated when shareholders voted down a similar proposal.

Sherborne boasts of having made its investors an average of 19 per cent a year, after fees, for the 12 years to the end of 2014. The performance of its target, Electra, has not been so consistent   but in the past year, according to FE Analytics data, Electra has made its investors 45 per cent.

This has led analysts and shareholders to question whether Electra is really in need of a rude awakening. But there are parallels between Mr Bramson s arguments and those which triumphed at Alliance Trust: he told shareholders in a letter on October 16 that costs were too high and the board was not independent enough from Electra Partners, which manages the investments.

Electra has made some changes since Mr Bramson began buying the trust, introducing a dividend policy and cutting costs. But Electra s management shot back on October 21:  It is deeply disappointing that Mr Bramson has shown such wilful ignorance concerning the success of Electra and how we operate. He has made a series of ill-judged, ill-informed and ill-founded claims about the company and its board   we cannot let this stand. 

Analysts at Numis believe the management may now lose the vote, since Mr Bramson has not only increased his stake but won support from some institutional investors.

But Charles Cade, head of investment company research, adds:  Whichever way the vote goes, the long term future of the fund appears uncertain, in our view, due to the divided nature of the shareholder base. 

Back to the future

Investment trusts have many qualities that open-ended funds will never have. Their capacity is limited by the number of shares in issue, so unlike mutual funds they cannot carry on gathering assets   and collecting percentage-based fees   indefinitely. They can accommodate illiquid assets such as property without worrying so much about how to sell them if investors desert the fund en masse.

But the listed structure comes with its own constraints, which have kept investment trusts a minority sport even as they become better known. Liquidity in trusts themselves can be a concern, and big wealth managers and adviser groups can shy away from recommending them because it may not be feasible for large numbers of clients to own the same fund.

Mr Newlands is a long-term fan of trusts but says:  Trusts under 200m are harder and harder to recommend, because investors need the reassurance they can get out as easily as they got into the position.

 We are increasingly aware of the importance of good liquidity and we wish to avoid concentration risk. 

Mr Brierley argues the sector is in need of more mergers, but questions whether this will happen given that boards which opt to merge are effectively putting themselves out of a job    six plus six does not equal 12 [on a board] .

Analysts hope that more direct competition with open-ended funds, especially now that investors can easily compare performance online, will keep up the pressure for lacklustre trusts to reform.

But the sector is also in a sweet spot as demand has increased, pushing share prices up and discounts to net asset value down.

 We all love to buy high-quality vehicles on high discounts, but there are not too many of those now,  says Mr Brierley.  You re having to work a lot harder now than back in 2009. 

Bargain basement?

When an investment trust is trading at a steep discount to net asset value, potential investors face a dilemma. If they buy, will they be getting a bargain or does the discount reflect poor prospects for the fund, perhaps because of a flawed investment strategy or an asset class under stress?

BlackRock s World Mining trust is a case in point. It has suffered badly from the downturn in the commodities and resources sectors, and took a steep writedown last October after the failure of one of its largest holdings, London Mining, a Sierra Leone iron ore miner. As a result, its share price has fallen almost 34 per cent in the past year and it trades at an 11 per cent discount.

But the fund made a series of changes in March, including a new fee structure, and it may now be time to reconsider BlackRock World Mining, according to analysts at Winterflood.  We believe that the fund remains an interesting play on a recovery in the resources sector,  they say.

Canaccord Genuity recommends a series of  opportunities for the contrarian , including Templeton Emerging Markets, an underperforming trust that also trades at an 11 per cent discount.

John Newlands, head of investment companies research at the wealth manager Brewin Dolphin, argues there may be opportunities in the private equity sector. He suggests looking at products such as Pantheon International Participations, a fund of funds trading at a 15 per cent discount.

 Private equity is a slightly damaged brand   a number of them suffered quite badly in the credit crunch when they were overcommitted and had to sell holdings almost at fire sale prices,  says Mr Newlands.

 But they ve learned their lesson and it s one of the few sectors where there are some attractive discounts left. 

Buyers must tread carefully, however. A big discount means the market is dubious, and investors should not take the plunge without understanding why.
